Kafalah — Surety
beginner
6 min
Definition and Formation of Kafala (Suretyship)
Linguistic and legal definition of kafala, types, and wording that forms it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of kafala, identify its two types, list valid formation words, and explain the ruling when the principal apostatizes in kafala bi al-nafs.

Core Idea
Kafala is a binding contract; the guarantor must produce the person, but impossibility due to apostasy grants a respite.

This lesson opens the chapter on kafala (suretyship/guarantee) by examining its linguistic and legal definition according to the Hanafi school. We will identify the words that form a valid kafala contract and then focus on a specific rule in kafala bi al-nafs: what happens when the principal apostatizes and flees to enemy territory. This ruling illustrates the Hanafi approach to obligations that become temporarily impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and legal definitions of kafala
Read the first part of the passage to find what kafala means both in language and in Hanafi law.
Linguistically, kafala means responsibility; legally, it is a contract of suretyship.
2
List the words that conclude kafala
Note the specific expression mentioned in the text that forms a valid kafala contract.
The passage explicitly states that 'adhamtuka' (I guarantee) concludes kafala.
3
Distinguish the two types of kafala
The text refers to kafala bi al-nafs (suretyship for the person) and implies kafala bi al-mal (suretyship for property).
The ruling that follows applies specifically to kafala bi al-nafs.
4
Analyze the obligation in kafala bi al-nafs
Identify the condition (specified time) and the necessary action for the guarantor.
The guarantor must produce the principal at the agreed time.
5
Apply the exception: apostasy
Understand the exceptional case where the principal apostatizes and joins dar al-harb, and note the consequence for the guarantor.
The guarantor is considered temporarily incapable and receives a respite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کفالت کا لغوی و شرعی معنی، کفالت کی قسمیں، کن الفاظ سے کفالت منعقد ہوتی ہے؟ لفظ أضمنتہ سے بھی کفالت منعقد ہو جاتی ہے۔ کتاب الکفالة: کفالت بالنفس میں مکفول بہ کو وقت متعین میں سپرد کرنے کی شرط ہے تو کفیل پر مکفول کو حاضر کرنا لازم ہے۔ مکفول بنفسہ شخص مرتد ہو گیا العیاذ باللہ اور دار الحرب میں لاحق ہو گیا تو کفیل اتنی مدت گویا عاجز ہے، اسے مہلت دی جائے گی۔
The linguistic and legal meaning of kafala, the types of kafala, with which words is kafala concluded? The word 'adhamtuka' (I guarantee) also concludes kafala. The Book of Kafala: In kafala bi al-nafs (suretyship for the person), there is a condition to deliver the subject of suretyship at a specified time, so it is necessary for the guarantor to produce the guaranteed person. If the guaranteed person himself apostatizes (God forbid) and joins dar al-harb, then the guarantor is considered incapable for that period; he will be granted a respite.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter of kafala (suretyship/guarantee). It begins by giving the linguistic and legal definition of kafala and mentions its types. It then lists the words by which a kafala contract is formed, noting that even the word 'adhamtuka' (I guarantee) is sufficient. The specific ruling presented is for kafala bi al-nafs (guarantee of the person): if the guarantor (kafil) has undertaken to produce the principal (makful bihi) at a specific time, then he is obliged to do so. However, if the principal apostatizes and flees to enemy territory (dar al-harb), the guarantor is considered unable to fulfill his obligation for the duration of that situation, and he is granted an extension of time until it becomes possible.

Key Terms
کفالت (kafala): A contract where a person (kafil) undertakes to produce the principal or pay a debt.
کفیل (kafil): The guarantor or surety.
مکفول بہ (makful bihi): The subject of suretyship; in kafala bi al-nafs it is the person being guaranteed, in kafala bi al-mal it is the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The guarantor is fully released from his obligation.
B) The guarantor is given a respite (extension).
C) The guarantor must still produce the principal immediately.
D) The guarantor must pay a penalty.

Key Takeaway
Kafala bi al-nafs obliges the guarantor to produce the principal at a specified time, but impossibility due to apostasy grants a temporary respite.

Kafalah — Surety
beginner

Overview of Kafala: Scope and Exceptions
List of topics and page numbers for the chapter on suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main categories of kafala (suretyship) and state the areas where kafala bi al-nafs is not permitted according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Kafala is a guarantee contract, but it is not valid in hudud and qisas because those are rights of Allah.

This lesson presents an overview of the Book of Kafala, outlining the key rulings and conditions that will be studied in detail. By understanding the scope of kafala—its types, discharge conditions, and exceptions—you will gain a roadmap for the entire chapter. This foundation is essential before diving into specific mas'ail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types of kafala
Distinguish between kafala bi al-nafs (suretyship for the person) and kafala bi al-mal (suretyship for property). Note the different rules for each.
Kafala bi al-nafs often requires producing the guaranteed person; kafala bi al-mal deals with financial liability.
2
Recognize conditions for discharge
List the ways a guarantor can be discharged: production of the person, death, payment, release by creditor, etc. Pay attention to stipulations like time and place.
The table of contents mentions delivery in court vs. market, and the effect of the guarantor's statements.
3
Note the exceptions to kafala bi al-nafs
State that kafala bi al-nafs is not permissible in hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation). Understand why: these are rights of Allah or involve personal inviolability.
This is a key limitation; no valid contract of guarantee exists in these areas.
4
Understand the role of the principal's command
Determine whether the guarantor acted with or without the command of the principal (makful anhu). This affects the right of recourse.
If by command, the guarantor can recover from the principal; otherwise, it is considered a donation.
5
Review additional rulings on multiple guarantors and settlements
Note that multiple guarantors are jointly liable and that compromise (sulh) or deferment (ta'jil) affects all parties.
These details will be expanded in the chapter.

Index of chapters and issues of kafala: What is the ruling if the subject of suretyship is produced in a place where the creditor can contest? The guarantor stipulated that he would deliver in the judge's court, then delivered in the market — will he be discharged or not? If the guarantor delivered the guaranteed person in a field or jungle, he will not be discharged. The guarantor is discharged from the surety by the death of the guaranteed person. He became a guarantor for another's person without saying 'when I give you, I am discharged'; he will be discharged by payment. The guarantor for the person stipulated: 'If I cannot produce him at such time, I am a guarantor' — the guarantee is upon the guaranteed person. The guarantor for the person said: 'If I cannot produce him tomorrow, he has money due' and the guaranteed died, then the guarantor will be guarantor for him. A person claimed one hundred dinars from another without mentioning its quality, and a guarantor for the person was made on condition that if he did not produce him, one hundred dinars would be due; when he did not produce him, one hundred dinars become due. Kafala bi al-nafs is not permissible in hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation); sayings of jurists. Kafala bi al-nafs by choice of the defendant is permissible by consensus. When can the defendant be imprisoned in hudud? Rahn (pledge) and kafala are permissible in kharaj (land tax). He appointed one guarantor for the person, then another; both will be guarantors. The ruling of kafala bi al-mal (suretyship for property). From whom should the creditor demand the money? If he demands from one, he still has the option to demand from the other. The ruling of attaching a condition to kafala. The guarantor said: 'Whatever you have due from another, I am a guarantor for it'; then one thousand dinars were proven by witnesses, so the guarantor will be guarantor for one thousand. Kafala is valid with or without the command of the principal. If the guarantor gave surety by the command of the principal, he will have recourse to him. The guarantor does not have the right to demand money from the principal before paying the debt. If the guarantor is arrested for money, he may arrest the principal. If the creditor releases the principal or receives payment, the guarantor is discharged. If the creditor deferred the debt from the principal, it is also deferred from the guarantor. If the guarantor compromised with the creditor for five hundred in lieu of one thousand, both the principal and guarantor are discharged.

Commentary
This page is a table of contents listing the various mas'ail (issues) of the Book of Kafala, along with page numbers. It covers a wide range: where the guaranteed person must be produced; the effect of the guarantor's stipulations; discharge by death or payment; multiple guarantors; the impermissibility of kafala in hudud and qisas; the permissibility of kafala by command; the right of recourse; compromise settlement; and deferment. This serves as a roadmap for the detailed rulings in the chapter.

Key Terms
حدود (hudud): Prescribed punishments for offenses like theft, adultery, etc.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment for murder or bodily harm.
مکفول عنہ (makful anhu): The principal on whose behalf the guarantee is given (the debtor or the person guaranteed).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Kafala bi al-nafs is valid in most cases but is not permitted in hudud and qisas because those are rights of Allah or involve personal inviolability.

Delivering the Guaranteed Person in Kafalah bil-Nafs
Rulings on where delivery of makful bihi discharges kafil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a guarantor (kafil bil-nafs) is discharged from his suretyship by delivering the guaranteed person (makful bihi), and identify when delivery is ineffective.

Core Idea
Delivery of the guaranteed person discharges the guarantor only if it enables the creditor to effectively litigate; delivery in a remote place does not.

In kafalah bil-nafs, the guarantor's obligation is to produce the guaranteed person when demanded. But not every production discharges him. This lesson clarifies the conditions that make delivery valid and when the obligation ends altogether.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
The act is delivery (taslim) of the makful bihi. The effect of valid delivery is discharge (bara'ah) of the kafil from his suretyship obligation.
2
List the conditions for valid delivery
Delivery must occur in a place where the creditor (makful lahu) can effectively litigate. If a specific venue was stipulated (e.g., judge's court), delivery elsewhere still discharges if the creditor can easily claim his right there.
The market example shows that stipulation does not nullify discharge if effective.
3
Identify invalid delivery scenarios
Delivery in a remote place like a field or forest does not discharge the guarantor, as the creditor cannot practically assert his right.
4
Identify the event that extinguishes the obligation
Death of the guaranteed person automatically discharges the guarantor, as the obligation becomes impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مکفول بہ کو ایسی جگہ حاضر کیا کہ مکفول لہ کو مخاصمت پر قدرت ہو تو کیا حکم ہے کفیل نے یہ شرط لگائی کہ وہ قاضی کی مجلس میں سپر د کرے گا پھر بازار میں سپرد کر دیا تو بری ہو جائے گا یا نہیں کفیل نے مکفول بنفسہ کو کسی میدان یا جنگل میں سپرد کیا تو کفیل بری نہ ہوگا مکفول بہ کی موت سے کفیل بالنفس کفالت سے بری ہو جاتا ہے
If the guaranteed person is produced in a place where the creditor has the ability to litigate, what is the ruling? The guarantor stipulated that he would hand him over in the judge's court, then handed him over in the market, will he be discharged or not? If the guarantor handed over the guaranteed person in a field or forest, the guarantor will not be discharged. With the death of the guaranteed person, the guarantor of the person is discharged from the suretyship.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on the delivery (taslim) of the makful bihi in kafalah bil-nafs. The first part queries the ruling when delivery is made in a place where the creditor can easily litigate. The second part deals with a condition: if the guarantor stipulated delivery in the judge's court but delivered elsewhere (market), the ruling is that he is discharged if the delivery is effective. However, the third part clarifies that delivery in a remote place like a field or forest does not discharge the guarantor because the creditor cannot practically claim his right there. Finally, if the guaranteed person dies, the guarantor is automatically discharged from the suretyship of the person, as the obligation is extinguished.

Key Terms
مکفول بہ (makful bihi): The person or property that is the subject of the guarantee (kafalah).
کفیل بالنفس (kafil bil-nafs): The guarantor who guarantees the presence (person) of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In kafalah bil-nafs, under which circumstance is the guarantor (kafil) NOT discharged according to this passage?
A) When the guaranteed person dies
B) When the guarantor delivers the person in a market
C) When the guarantor delivers the person in a field or forest
D) When the guarantor delivers the person in the judge's court

Key Takeaway
The guarantor in kafalah bil-nafs is discharged either by delivering the guaranteed person in a place where the creditor can effectively litigate, or by the death of the guaranteed person.

Discharge and Conditional Guarantee in Kafalah bil-Nafs
Rulings on conditional kafalah and discharge by payment or condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two rulings: (1) a kafil bil-nafs is discharged by actual delivery even without explicit condition; (2) a conditional kafalah remains a guarantee of the person, not the debt.

Core Idea
Delivery discharges without explicit condition; a conditional guarantee remains guarantee of person, not debt.

Kafalah bil-nafs (guarantee of a person's presence) has specific rules. This passage addresses two practical scenarios: first, whether explicit wording is needed for discharge upon delivery; second, whether a condition attached to the guarantee changes its legal nature. Understanding these distinctions is crucial to correctly classify the liability in such guarantees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two separate rulings. First, a person becomes a kafil bil-nafs without saying 'I am discharged when I deliver'. Second, the kafil attaches a condition: 'if I cannot produce him by a certain time, I am guarantor'.
2
Analyze scenario 1: discharge by delivery
The ruling states that even without an explicit condition, actual delivery (ada) discharges the kafil. The key point is that discharge occurs by performance, not by wording.
This follows the Hanafi principle that substance prevails over form in contractual obligations.
3
Analyze scenario 2: conditional guarantee
The conditional statement does not change the guarantee's nature. It remains a guarantee of the person (kafalah bil-nafs), not a guarantee of debt. The responsibility stays on the guaranteed person himself.
This clarifies that conditions do not transform the type of guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی دوسرے کا کفیل بالنفس ہوا یہ نہیں کہا کہ جب میں تم کو دے دوں تو بری ہوں ادا کرنے سے بری ہو جائے گا کفیل بالنفس نے یہ شرط لگائی کہ اگر فلاں وقت حاضر نہ کر سکا تو میں ضامن ہوں گا ضمان مکفول بنفسہ پر ہوگا
Someone became a guarantor of the person of another, and did not say 'When I give you, I am discharged.' He will be discharged by payment. The guarantor of the person stipulated that if he cannot produce him by a certain time, then I will be a guarantor. The guarantee will be on the guaranteed person.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage covers two different scenarios. First, if a person becomes a kafil bil-nafs without explicitly stating that delivery will discharge him, he is still discharged by actually delivering the guaranteed person (payment/production). Second, if the kafil bil-nafs attaches a condition to his guarantee (e.g., 'if I cannot produce him by a certain time, I am a guarantor'), this condition is valid, and the guarantee becomes a guarantee of the person (not the debt). The responsibility remains on the guaranteed person himself. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that kafalah bil-nafs is a guarantee of the person, not the debt, and conditions that specify a time or event are valid as long as they do not alter the essential nature of the guarantee.

Key Terms
ضامن (dhamin): A guarantor who assumes liability for a debt or the presence of a person.
ادا کرنا (ada karna): Payment or delivery that fulfills the obligation and discharges the guarantor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if a kafil bil-nafs says 'if I cannot produce him by tomorrow, I am a guarantor,' what is the nature of the guarantee?
A) It becomes a guarantee of debt
B) It is invalid
C) It remains a guarantee of the person
D) The kafil is immediately liable

Key Takeaway
In kafalah bil-nafs, delivery discharges even without explicit condition; a conditional guarantee remains a guarantee of the person, not debt.

Kafalah bil-Mal: Multiple Guarantors and Discharge
Rulings on multiple guarantors and discharge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on multiple sureties in kafalah bil-mal and explain how discharge occurs through payment, deferment, or compromise.

Core Idea
In kafalah bil-mal, the creditor may demand from any surety, and discharge of the principal debtor or compromise by the surety discharges both.

This lesson covers key rulings in kafalah bil-mal (suretyship for property) from the Hanafi school. You will learn the legal effects of having multiple guarantors, the creditor's rights, and the various ways the surety's obligation ends. Understanding these principles is essential for applying suretyship contracts correctly in financial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties in kafalah bil-mal
Recognize the three parties: creditor (makful lahu), principal debtor (makful 'anhu), and surety/guarantor (kafil). The surety guarantees payment of a debt owed by the principal debtor to the creditor.
In this passage, multiple sureties are appointed one after another.
2
Understand the effect of multiple sureties
The passage states that if one surety is appointed and then another, both are sureties. The creditor can demand payment from any of them and still retain the right to demand from the others.
This shows that each surety is individually liable for the full debt; joint suretyship with several liability.
3
Learn how discharge of the principal debtor affects the surety
If the creditor discharges the principal debtor (e.g., forgives the debt) or receives payment from him, the surety is automatically discharged. The obligation is extinguished.
Discharge of the principal debtor also releases the surety because the debt no longer exists.
4
Recognize the impact of deferment granted to the principal debtor
If the creditor grants a deferment (ta'khir) to the principal debtor, the same deferment applies to the surety. The creditor cannot demand immediate payment from the surety while the deferment is in effect.
The surety benefits from the same time extension as the principal debtor.
5
Analyze the effect of compromise (sulh) by the surety
If the surety settles the debt with the creditor for a lesser amount by way of sulh (compromise), both the principal debtor and the surety are discharged from the original debt. The creditor cannot pursue the balance from either.
This is a comprehensive discharge: the sulh extinguishes the entire original obligation.

ایک کفیل بالنفس بنایا پھر دوسرا بنایا دونوں کفیل ہوں گے کفالت بالمال کا حکم مكفول لہ مال کا کس سے مطالبہ کرے ایک سے مطالبہ کیا تو دوسرے سے بھی مطالبہ کا اختیار ہے مکفول لہ مکفول عنہ کو بری کر دے یا وصول کر لے تو کفیل بری ہو جائیگا مکفول لہ نے مکفول عنہ سے قرض مؤخر کر دیا تو کفیل سے بھی مؤخر ہو جائے گا کفیل نے رب المال سے ہزار کے عوض پانچ سو پر صلح کر لی تو مکفول عنہ اور کفیل دونوں بری ہوں گے
One guarantor of the person was appointed, then another was appointed; both will be guarantors. The ruling of suretyship for property: From whom should the creditor demand the property? If he demanded from one, he still has the option to demand from the other. If the creditor discharges the principal debtor or receives payment, the guarantor will be discharged. If the creditor deferred the debt for the principal debtor, it is also deferred for the guarantor. If the guarantor compromised with the creditor for five hundred in lieu of a thousand, both the principal debtor and the guarantor are discharged.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings in kafalah bil-mal (suretyship for property). First, if multiple sureties are appointed one after another, all are sureties—each can be held liable. The creditor can choose to demand payment from any one of them and still retain the right to demand from others. Second, if the creditor discharges the principal debtor (makful 'anhu) or actually receives payment from him, the guarantor is automatically discharged. Third, if the creditor grants a deferment (ta'khir) to the principal debtor, the same deferment applies to the guarantor; the creditor cannot demand immediate payment from the guarantor. Fourth, if the guarantor settles the debt with the creditor for a lesser amount (e.g., five hundred instead of a thousand) by way of sulh (compromise), both the principal debtor and the guarantor are discharged from the remaining debt. This shows the interconnected nature of the obligations in kafalah bil-mal.

Key Terms
مکفول عنہ (makful 'anhu): The principal debtor; the person on whose behalf the guarantee is given.
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement agreement between parties, often for a reduced amount.
کفیل (kafil): The guarantor or surety who assumes liability for the debt of another.
مکفول لہ (makful lahu): The creditor or beneficiary of the suretyship; the one to whom the debt is owed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a guarantor (kafil) in kafalah bil-mal compromises with the creditor for a lesser amount, what happens to the principal debtor?
A) He remains liable for the original debt
B) He is also discharged
C) He must pay the difference
D) The compromise is invalid without his consent

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafalah bil-mal, multiple sureties are each fully liable, and discharge of the principal debtor, deferment, or compromise by a surety extinguishes the obligation for all parties involved.

Introduction to Kafalah: Definition, Placement, and Key Rulings
Introduces Kafalah: definition, placement, parties, conditions, and a minor ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of kafalah, identify the parties, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the requirement of acceptance.

Core Idea
Kafalah joins one liability to another for demand, not just debt — this includes personal guarantees.

This lesson introduces the book of Kafalah from Ashraf al-Hidayah. You will learn why the jurists placed it after sales, the precise technical definition, the roles of the parties, and a subtle disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf on whether acceptance by the creditor is necessary. Understanding this foundation is critical before studying detailed rulings.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the placement rationale
Consider why the author placed kafalah after the chapters of sales (buyu'). This teaches us about the coherence of fiqh arrangement. The need for guarantees typically arises after transactions, and both are compensatory contracts.
2
Compare the two technical definitions
Read the two definitions: (1) joining liability to liability in demand, (2) joining liability to liability in debt. The author prefers the first. Understand why it is broader and includes personal guarantees.
The Quranic verse (Wa kafalaha Zakariya) is cited linguistically, not as a proof for the technical definition.
3
Identify the four parties
From the example: kafil (guarantor), makful 'anhu (principal), makful lahu (beneficiary), makful bihi (guaranteed thing). Note that 'madmun bihi' is synonymous with makful bihi.
4
Extract the conditions and eligibility
List the conditions: kafil must have power to deliver, the debt (if any) must be valid, kafil must be eligible for dispositions (sane adult).
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on acceptance
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) require both ijab and qabul; Abu Yusuf holds that only the kafil's ijab suffices. Consider the thamarah (practical consequence) and which view is mufta bihi.
This dispute is within the Hanafi school; the mufta bihi is the majority view.

تشریح فاضل مصنف نے کتاب الکفالہ کو ابواب بیوع کے بعد اس لئے ذکر کیا ہے کہ کفالہ کی ضرورت بالعموم بیوع کے بعد پڑتی ہے کیونکہ کبھی بائع مشتری سے مطمئن نہیں ہوتا تو ایسے آدمی کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے جو مشتری کی جانب سے شمن کا کفیل ہو جائے اور کبھی مشتری ، بائع سے مطمئن نہیں ہوتا تو ایسے شخص کی ضرورت پیش آتی ہے جو بائع کی جانب سے مبیع کا کفیل ہو جائے ۔ پس جب کفالہ کی ضرورت بالعموم بیع کے بعد پیش آتی ہے تو کفالہ کے احکام کو ابواب بیوع کے بعد ذکر کیا گیا ہے۔ دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ کفالہ اگر مکفول عنہ کے امر سے ہو تو وہ انتہاء معاوضہ ہوتا ہے اور بیع بھی عقد معاوضہ ہے اسلئے نیوع سے پہلے کفالہ کو ذکر کیا گیا ہے۔ کفیل ، کفالت کرنے والا ۔ مکفول عنہ۔ جس کی طرف سے کفالت کی گئی ہمکفول لہ جس کے واسطے کفالت کی گئی ، مکفول بہ جس چیز کی کفالت کی گئی ۔ مثلاً حامد نے خالد کی طرف سے شاہد کے واسطے ایک ہزار روپیہ کی کفالت کی ۔ تو حامد کفیل، خالد مکفول عنہ، شاہد مکغول لہ اور مکفول یہ کی جگہ مضمون یہ کہتے ہیں۔

کفالت کا لغوی و شرعی معنی
قال الكفالة هى الضم لغة قال الله تعالى وكفلها زكريا ثم قيل هى ضم الذمة الى الذمه في المطالبة وقيل في الدين والاول اصح ترجمه کفالت کے معنی لغت میں ملانے کے ہیں۔ اللہ تعالیٰ نے فرمایا ہے اور زکریا نے مریم کو اپنے ساتھ ملا لیا ہے۔ پھر کہا گیا کہ مطالبہ میں ذمہ کو ذمہ سے ملانا ہے اور کہا گیا کہ دین میں اور قول اول زیادہ صحیح ہے۔
تشریح صاحب قدوری نے کفالہ کے معنی بیان کرتے ہوئے فرمایا کہ کفالت کے لغوی معنی ضم اور ملانے کے ہیں۔ چنانچہ باری تعالیٰ کا ارشاد ہے۔ و كفلها زکریا یعنی حضرت زکریا علیہ السلام حضرت مریم کے کفیل ہو گئے ۔ یعنی حضرت زکریا علیہ السلام نے حضرت مریم علیہا السلام کو اپنی پرورش میں ملالیا۔ اہل شرع نے کفالہ کی دو تعریفیں کی ہیں۔ ایک ضم الذمة الى الذمة في المطالبة یعنی ایک ذمہ کو دوسرے ذمہ سے مطالبہ میں ملانا۔ دوم ضم الذمة الى الذمة فی الدین یعنی ایک ذمہ کو دوسرے ذمہ سے دین میں ملانا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ قول اول زیادہ صحیح ہے کیونکہ دوسری تعریف کے مطابق کفالہ کیلئے دین ضروری ہے حالانکہ کفالہ جس طرح مال کا درست ہے اسی طرح نفس کا کفالہ بھی صحیح ہے اور کفالہ بالنفس میں مال نہیں ہوتا۔ پس دوسری تعریف کی بناء پر کفالہ بانفس کفالہ کی
Ashraf al-Hidaya Volume Nine 131. Book of Kafalah. Translation: This book is about the rulings of kafalah. Explanation: The author mentioned the book of kafalah after the chapters of sales because the need for kafalah generally arises after sales. Sometimes the seller is not satisfied with the buyer, so a person is needed who becomes the guarantor for the price on behalf of the buyer. Sometimes the buyer is not satisfied with the seller, so a person is needed who becomes the guarantor for the sold item on behalf of the seller. So since the need for kafalah generally arises after sales, the rulings of kafalah are mentioned after the chapters of sales. A second reason is that if kafalah is by the order of the principal (mukful 'anhu), then it is a compensatory transaction, and sale is also a compensatory transaction, so kafalah is mentioned after sales. Kafil: the one who guarantees. Mukful 'anhu: the one on whose behalf the guarantee is made. Mukful lahu: the one for whom the guarantee is made. Mukful bihi: the thing for which the guarantee is made. For example, Hamid guaranteed one thousand rupees on behalf of Khalid for Shahid. So Hamid is the kafil, Khalid is the mukful 'anhu, Shahid is the mukful lahu, and in place of mukful bihi they say madmun bihi. Kafalah, according to the two sides (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), is offer and acceptance, and according to Imam Abu Yusuf, only the offer from the kafil suffices, whether acceptance occurs or not. Its condition is that the kafil has the power to hand over the guaranteed thing, and the debt is a valid debt. And the one eligible for kafalah is every person who is eligible for dispositions. And the ruling of kafalah is that demanding (payment) from the kafil becomes obligatory. (Jamil) The lexical and technical meaning of kafalah. He said: Kafalah linguistically means 'ضم' (joining). Allah ta'ala said: 'And Zakariya took her into his care (kafalaha)'. Then it was said: it is the joining of liability to liability in demand. And it was said: in debt. And the first is more correct. Translation: The meaning of kafalah lexically is 'to join'. Allah ta'ala said: And Zakariya joined Maryam to himself. Then it was said: it is joining one liability to another in demand; and it was said: in debt. And the first opinion is more correct. Explanation: Sahib al-Quduri, when explaining the meaning of kafalah, said that its lexical meaning is 'ضم' and joining. So Allah ta'ala said: 'Wa kafalaha Zakariya' meaning Zakariya (peace be upon him) became the kafil of Maryam, i.e., he took her under his care. The jurists have given two definitions of kafalah. One: joining the liability to liability in demand. Second: joining the liability to liability in debt. The author of al-Hidayah says that the first opinion is more correct because according to the second definition, a debt is necessary for kafalah, whereas kafalah is valid for property as well as for the person (kafalah bil nafs), and kafalah bil nafs does not involve property. So according to the second definition, kafalah bil nafs would not be included.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to the book of Kafalah (Suretyship/Guarantee) from the commentary Ashraf al-Hidaya. The author first explains why the chapter on kafalah is placed after the chapters on sales (buyu'): because the practical need for kafalah typically arises after sales, such as when a seller is not confident about the buyer's payment and requires a guarantor for the price, or when a buyer wants assurance of delivery and requires a guarantor for the sold item. A second rationale is that if the guarantee is made at the request of the principal debtor, it has the nature of a compensatory contract, similar to sale. The passage then defines the key parties: al-kafil (guarantor), al-makful 'anhu (principal debtor), al-makful lahu (beneficiary/creditor), and al-makful bihi (the guaranteed obligation), noting that the term 'madmun bihi' is also used for the guaranteed thing. Next, the author states that kafalah, according to the majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani), requires both offer (ijab) from the guarantor and acceptance (qabul) by the beneficiary. However, Imam Abu Yusuf holds that only the guarantor's offer is sufficient, regardless of whether acceptance occurs. This is a noted intra-Hanafi dispute. Conditions for valid kafalah include the guarantor's ability to deliver the guaranteed item (e.g., the debt or the person) and that the underlying debt is valid. The guarantor must be legally capable of transactions (i.e., sane and adult). The ruling is that the guarantor becomes obligated to pay upon demand (the creditor has the right to demand from the guarantor directly). The passage then delves into the linguistic and technical definition of kafalah. Linguistically, it means 'ضم' (joining), confirmed by the Quranic verse 'Wa kafalaha Zakariya' (Surah Aal-e-Imran: 37) where Zakariya took Maryam into his care. Technically, two definitions are given: (1) joining one liability to another in demand (ضم الذمة الى الذمة في المطالبة), and (2) joining one liability to another in debt. The author prefers the first definition, relying on the authority of al-Hidayah, because the second definition restricts kafalah to matters involving debt, whereas the Hanafi school recognizes kafalah bil nafs (personal surety, e.g., for the appearance of a person in court) which does not involve a monetary debt. Thus the first, broader definition is more comprehensive and accurate.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): Linguistically: 'ضم' (joining). Technically: joining one liability to another for the purpose of demand (ضم الذمة إلى الذمة في المطالبة).
كفيل (kafīl): The person who becomes a guarantor for another's obligation.
مكفول عنه (makfūl 'anhu): The principal debtor, i.e., the person on whose behalf the guarantee is made.
مكفول له (makfūl lahu): The beneficiary/creditor, i.e., the person for whom the guarantee is made.
مكفول به (makfūl bihi): The thing or obligation that is guaranteed, e.g., a debt or the presence of a person.
مضمون به (madmūn bihi): Synonym for مكفول به, the guaranteed obligation.
ضم الذمة إلى الذمة (ḍamm al-dhimmah ilā al-dhimmah): Joining one responsibility/liability to another, so that the kafil becomes jointly liable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred definition in the Hanafi school, what does kafalah mean technically?
A) The joining of two liabilities in debt only.
B) The joining of one liability to another for the purpose of demand.
C) The transfer of a debt from one person to another.
D) The appointment of a representative to pay a debt.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah in the Hanafi school is defined as joining liability to liability for demand, encompassing both financial and personal guarantees, and its validity requires both offer and acceptance according to the majority.

Kafalah — Surety

Formation of Kafalah: Ijab, Qabul, and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah formation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid kafalah contract and distinguish between the positions of the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the requirement of acceptance (qabul).

Core Idea
A surety contract requires both offer and acceptance unless one follows Imam Abu Yusuf.

This passage from al-Quduri's matn lays out the essential elements of a kafalah contract. The two sides of the Hanafi school – the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) on one hand and Imam Abu Yusuf on the other – agree on most conditions but differ on whether the creditor's acceptance (qabul) is required. This difference has practical implications for the validity of suretyship in everyday transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Recognize that the core dispute is whether qabul (acceptance by the creditor) is a necessary element for the formation of kafalah.
The passage states that according to the two sides (Sahibayn), both ijab and qabul are required; according to Abu Yusuf, only ijab suffices.
2
State the Sahibayn position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that kafalah is a contract that requires mutual consent, so both an offer from the surety and an acceptance from the creditor are necessary.
This is the majority view within the Hanafi school and is generally considered mufta bihi.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Imam Abu Yusuf argues that only the surety's offer (ijab) is sufficient; acceptance is not a condition. The contract is formed by the surety's declaration alone.
The basis for this view is not given in the text, but it is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
4
List the agreed conditions
The passage lists three conditions: the surety must have the ability to hand over the subject matter (makful bih), the debt must be valid (dayn sahih), and the surety must be legally capable of transactions (ahliyyat al-tasarrufat).
These conditions are accepted by all three imams.
5
Determine the ruling effect
The ruling (hukm) of a valid kafalah is that the surety becomes liable to be demanded – either to pay the debt or produce the person guaranteed.
This obligation (wujub al-mutalabah) is the primary effect of the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کفالہ کارکن طرفین کے نزدیک ایجاب و قبول ہے او امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک فقط کفیل کی طرف سے ایجاب ہے۔ قبول پایا جائے یا نہ پایا جائے اور اس کی شرط یہ ہے کہ کفیل ، مکفول به سپرد کرنے پر قدرت رکھتا ہو اور دین، دین صحیح ہو ۔ اور کفالہ کا اہل ہر وہ آدمی ہے جو تصرفات کا اہل ہے اور کفالہ کا حکم کفیل پر مطالبہ کا واجب ہوتا ہے۔
Kafalah according to the two sides (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) consists of offer and acceptance, and according to Imam Abu Yusuf, only offer from the surety (kafil) suffices, whether acceptance occurs or not. And its condition is that the surety is able to hand over the subject matter (makful bih), and the debt is a valid debt. And every person who is capable of legal dispositions is eligible for suretyship. And the ruling of suretyship is that the demand (for payment or presence) becomes obligatory upon the surety.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This is a concise ruling from the matn (al-Quduri) on the formation of a kafalah contract. The 'two sides' refers to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, who hold that both offer (ijab from the surety) and acceptance (qabul from the creditor) are necessary. Imam Abu Yusuf holds that only the surety's offer is needed, and acceptance is not a condition. The text also lists conditions: the surety must be able to hand over the guaranteed item/person, and the debt must be valid. Eligibility requires capacity to transact. The effect is that the surety becomes liable to be demanded (either to pay or to produce the person). This is a clear intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and the mufta bihi position is not specified here, but typically the view of the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is relied upon.

Key Terms
ایجاب (ijab): Offer made by the surety to become responsible
قبول (qabul): Acceptance by the creditor (makful lahu) of the suretyship

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf, what is sufficient for a valid kafalah?
A) Both ijab and qabul
B) Only ijab from the surety
C) Only qabul from the creditor
D) A written contract

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a valid kafalah contract requires both offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) according to the majority (Sahibayn), but only offer according to Imam Abu Yusuf.

Kafalah — Surety

Definition of Kafalah: Linguistic and Legal
Defines kafalah linguistically and legally, with tarjih between two definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two competing legal definitions of kafalah and explain why the Hanafi school prefers the definition based on demand (al-mutalaba) over the definition based on debt (al-dayn).

Core Idea
Kafalah means joining one liability to another in demand — not just in debt — ensuring it covers both property and personal suretyship.

This lesson unpacks the definition of kafalah (suretyship) as presented in al-Hidayah. The author, al-Marghinani, examines two juristic definitions and selects the more comprehensive one. By parsing the linguistic root and the legal reasoning, you will understand why the Hanafi school’s definition is broader and more accurate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic root
Find the linguistic meaning of kafalah (al-damm, joining) and the Quranic evidence cited (Surah Aal-e-Imran 3:37).
2
Recognize the two legal definitions
Note the two definitions: (1) joining liability to liability in demand (al-mutalaba), and (2) joining liability to liability in debt (al-dayn).
3
Analyze the critique of the debt definition
Understand why the debt definition is problematic: it excludes kafalah bi al-nafs (personal suretyship) and implies two debts where only one exists.
The first issue is exclusivity, the second is logical duplication.
4
Understand the preference for the demand definition
Realize that the demand definition is more comprehensive, covering both property and personal suretyship, and avoids the duplicate debt problem.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

قال الكفالة هى الضم لغة قال الله تعالى وكفلها زكريا ثم قيل هى ضم الذمة الى الذمه في المطالبة وقيل في الدين والاول اصح ترجمه کفالت کے معنی لغت میں ملانے کے ہیں۔ اللہ تعالیٰ نے فرمایا ہے اور زکریا نے مریم کو اپنے ساتھ ملا لیا ہے۔ پھر کہا گیا کہ مطالبہ میں ذمہ کو ذمہ سے ملانا ہے اور کہا گیا کہ دین میں اور قول اول زیادہ صحیح ہے۔ تشریح صاحب قدوری نے کفالہ کے معنی بیان کرتے ہوئے فرمایا کہ کفالت کے لغوی معنی ضم اور ملانے کے ہیں۔ چنانچہ باری تعالیٰ کا ارشاد ہے۔ و كفلها زکریا یعنی حضرت زکریا علیہ السلام حضرت مریم کے کفیل ہو گئے ۔ یعنی حضرت زکریا علیہ السلام نے حضرت مریم علیہا السلام کو اپنی پرورش میں ملالیا۔ اہل شرع نے کفالہ کی دو تعریفیں کی ہیں۔ ایک ضم الذمة الى الذمة في المطالبة یعنی ایک ذمہ کو دوسرے ذمہ سے مطالبہ میں ملانا۔ دوم ضم الذمة الى الذمة فی الدین یعنی ایک ذمہ کو دوسرے ذمہ سے دین میں ملانا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ قول اول زیادہ صحیح ہے کیونکہ دوسری تعریف کے مطابق کفالہ کیلئے دین ضروری ہے حالانکہ کفالہ جس طرح مال کا درست ہے اسی طرح نفس کا کفالہ بھی صحیح ہے اور کفالہ بالنفس میں مال نہیں ہوتا۔ پس دوسری تعریف کی بناء پر کفالہ بانفس کفالہ کی تعریف سے خارج ہو جائے گا ۔ حالانکہ کفالہ بالنفس بھی کفالہ ہے۔ دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ دوسری تعریف کی بناء پر کفیل کے ذمہ میں دین ثابت ہو جاتا ہے اور جب کفیل کے ذمہ میں دین ثابت ہو گیا اور اصیل بری نہیں ہوا تو دین دو ہو گئے حالانکہ دین فقط ایک تھا۔ پس ان وجود سے تعریف اول کو صحیح قرار دیا گیا ہے۔
He said: Kafalah linguistically means 'joining' (al-damm). Allah said: 'And Zakariya took charge of her (kafalaha).' Then it is said: It is joining a liability (dhimma) to another liability in demanding (the subject). And it is said: (joining) in debt. The first is more correct. Translation: Kafalah linguistically means to join. Allah says: 'And Zakariya took Maryam into his care.' Then it is said: It is joining one dhimma to another in demand, and it is said: in debt. The first saying is more correct. Explanation: Sahib al-Quduri said regarding the meaning of kafalah: Its linguistic meaning is al-damm (joining). Thus Allah's statement: 'Wa kafalaha Zakariyya' meaning Zakariya became the kafil of Maryam, i.e., he took her into his upbringing. The jurists have given two definitions: first, joining liability to liability in the demand (for payment or presence); second, joining liability to liability in debt. The author of al-Hidayah states that the first is more correct because according to the second definition, a debt is necessary for kafalah, while kafalah is valid both for property and for the person (kafalah bi al-nafs). Kafalah bi al-nafs does not involve debt, so it would be excluded from the definition. Moreover, according to the second definition, a debt is established in the surety's liability while the principal remains liable, resulting in two debts whereas there was only one. Therefore, due to these reasons, the first definition is deemed correct.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This is a definitional passage from al-Hidayah where the author (al-Marghinani) clarifies the meaning of kafalah. The linguistic root is al-damm (to join), supported by the Quranic verse about Zakariya becoming the guardian of Maryam. Legally, there are two definitions: (1) joining one liability to another in the context of demand (for property or presence), and (2) joining in debt only. The author prefers the first because the second would exclude kafalah bi al-nafs (suretyship for a person's presence) since there is no debt there. Also, if debt were the basis, it would create two debts simultaneously (the principal's and the surety's) which is problematic. Thus, the more comprehensive definition is chosen.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Joining one liability to another in the context of demand for a subject (property or person)
ضم الذمة إلى الذمة (damm al-dhimma ila al-dhimma): Adding a second liability (the surety's) to the original liability (the principal's) regarding the demand

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Hidayah prefer the first definition of kafalah (joining in demand) over the second (joining in debt)?
A) Because it is simpler
B) Because it includes kafalah bi al-nafs
C) Because it was used by the Prophet
D) Because it avoids making two debts

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of kafalah as joining liability in demand (al-mutalaba) is preferred because it covers both property and personal suretyship, avoiding the problems of the narrower debt-based definition.

Kafalah — Surety

Types of Kafalah and the Hanafi Position on Personal Suretyship
Divides kafalah into two types and argues with Shafi'i about personal suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between kafalah bi al-nafs and kafalah bi al-mal, and explain the Hanafi proof for the validity of personal suretyship, including the rebuttal to the Shafi'i weak opinion.

Core Idea
Personal suretyship is valid because the hadith 'al-za'im gharim' covers both types and delivery is possible through legal means.

This passage from al-Hidayah divides kafalah into two fundamental types and presents the Hanafi position on personal suretyship, along with a rebuttal of a weak Shafi'i opinion. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi approach to contractual guarantees and the use of general proofs (umum al-dalil) to establish validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the first line: 'al-kafalah darban: kafalah bi al-nafs wa kafalah bi al-mal'. Understand that the division is based on the subject of the guarantee: the person or the property.
The term 'darban' indicates two categories, not more.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on personal suretyship
The text says 'al-kafalah bi al-nafs ja'izah' – it is permissible and valid. The obligation is to produce the principal (iḥḍār al-makfūl bih).
'Ja'izah' here means valid and enforceable.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i (weak opinion) argues that the surety cannot deliver a free person because he has no authority over him. He analogizes to financial suretyship where the surety has control over his own property.
This is a qiyas on inability to deliver, but Hanafis reject it.
4
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'al-za'im gharim' (the guarantor is liable). This is a general proof covering all types of kafalah. Also, practical delivery is possible by informing the claimant of the principal's location or seeking court help.
The hadith is used as a primary text (nass) and the practical ability is a rational argument.
5
Appreciate the need (hajah)
The text mentions 'al-hajah massah ilayhi' – there is a great need for personal suretyship (e.g., in criminal cases). This social necessity supports its legality.
Consideration of need (hajah) is a valid basis for ruling in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الكفالة ضربان كفالة بالنفس وكفالة بالمال فالكفالة بالنفس جائزة والمضمون بها احضار المكفول به و قال الشافعى لا يجوز لانه كفل بما لا يقدر على تسليمه اذ لا قدرة له على نفس المكفول به بخلاف الكفالة بالمال لان له ولاية على مال نفسه ولنا قوله عليه السلام الزعيم غارم وهذا يفيد مشروعية الكفالة بنوعيها ولانه يقدر على تسليمه بطريقه بان يعلم الطالب مكانه فيخلی بینه و بینه او يستعين باعوان القاضي في ذلك والحاجة ماسة اليه وقد امكن تحقيق معنى الكفالة فيه وهو الضم في المطالبة
He said: Kafalah is of two types: personal suretyship (kafalah bi al-nafs) and financial suretyship (kafalah bi al-mal). Personal suretyship is permissible, and what is guaranteed by it is the production of the person (the makful bih). Imam al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible because he guaranteed something he is unable to deliver, as he has no power over the person of the principal. Unlike financial suretyship, because he has authority over his own property. Our proof is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'The guarantor is liable' (al-za'im gharim). This indicates the legality of both types of suretyship. Also, he is able to deliver him by a method: by informing the claimant of his location, thus enabling him to confront him, or by seeking the help of the judge's assistants in that. And there is a great need for it, and the meaning of kafalah (joining in demand) is achieved in it.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This is a passage from al-Hidayah presenting the division of kafalah and the inter-madhhab dispute on personal suretyship. The Hanafi school (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds that kafalah bi al-nafs is valid. The word 'al-madmun biha' means that the surety is responsible for producing the person. Imam al-Shafi'i is reported to have a weak opinion that it is invalid because the surety cannot physically control the free person. The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'al-za'im gharim' which is general, and also the practical ability to deliver the person by informing the authorities. The need for such suretyship (e.g., in criminal cases) further supports its validity. Note that Shafi'i's 'qawl da'if' (weak opinion) is recorded; his stronger view (al-azhar) is actually in agreement with the Hanafis, as mentioned in the commentary.

Key Terms
كفالة بالنفس (kafalah bi al-nafs): Suretyship for the person (presence) of the principal; the surety guarantees to bring him
زعيم غارم (za'im gharim): The guarantor is liable/responsible

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi proof for the validity of kafalah bi al-nafs?
A) Qiyas on kafalah bi al-mal
B) The hadith 'al-za'im gharim'
C) Consensus of scholars
D) The Quranic verse on Zakariya

Key Takeaway
Personal suretyship (kafalah bi al-nafs) is valid in the Hanafi school, supported by the general hadith 'al-za'im gharim' and the ability to deliver the principal through legal means.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
6 min
Proofs for Kafalah bi al-Nafs: Hadith, Practicality, and Qiyas
Proving kafalah bi al-nafs through hadith and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three proofs for the validity of kafalah bi al-nafs in Hanafi fiqh and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
Kafalah bi al-nafs is valid because the surety can hand over the person, just like property.

The Hanafi school holds that kafalah bi al-nafs—guaranteeing the appearance of a person—is valid. This lesson examines the three proofs from the Hidayah: a general hadith, the practical capability of the surety, and analogy (qiyas) to kafalah bi al-mal. Understanding these proofs clarifies why the Hanafi position differs from the Shafi'i view and how the obligation is enforceable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its classification
What is the fiqh ruling on kafalah bi al-nafs according to the Hanafi school? Recognize it as permissible (ja'iz/mubah) based on the proofs given.
The basic ruling is permissibility; it may become obligatory in certain contexts.
2
Analyze the first proof: the hadith
Examine the hadith 'al-za'eem gharim'—how does its generality (itlaq) indicate both types of kafalah?
The word 'za'eem' is general; the predicate 'gharim' confirms liability.
3
Examine the second proof: refuting the impossibility claim
Understand the Shafi'i objection that the surety cannot physically hand over the person, and then the Hanafi response: the surety can indicate the location or seek judicial assistance.
This shows practical possibility, not physical control.
4
Understand the third proof: qiyas
Trace the analogy: just as kafalah bi al-mal is needed and valid, so is kafalah bi al-nafs. Both involve joining liability (damm al-dhimmah).
Identify the common illah: the need for security and the ability to join liability.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
How does this dispute affect actual practice? In Hanafi fiqh, a surety can be held liable for non-appearance; in Shafi'i, such a guarantee is unenforceable.
This difference matters in contracts and court proceedings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشرف الهدایہ جلد نہم
كتاب الكفاله
پوری پوری ولایت حاصل ہے اور جب کفیل کو اپنے مال پر ولایت حاصل ہے تو اس کو سپرد کرنے پر قادر بھی ہوگا۔ اور جب کفیل مال سپرد کرنے پر قادر ہے تو کفالہ بالمال جائز ہوگا۔
ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ رسول ہاشمی صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا ہے الزعيم غارم یعنی کفیل ضامن ہے۔ یہ حدیث اپنے اطلاق کی وجہ سے کفالہ کی دونوں قسموں کی مشروعیت کا فائدہ دیتی ہے۔
دوسری دلیل اور امام شافعی کی دلیل کا جواب یہ ہے کہ یہ کہنا کہ کفیل [پیکول؟] بہ کی ذات سپرد کرنے پر قادر نہیں ہے۔ غلط ہے بلکہ کفیل مکفول بہ کی ذات سپرد کرنے پر قادر ہے۔ بایں طور کہ کفیل اپنے مکفول لیہ کو اس شخص کو ٹھکانا جتلا دے جس کی اس نے ذاتی کفالت کی ہے یعنی طالب یعنی مکفول لہ اور مطلوب یعنی مکفول بنفسہ کے درمیان تخلیہ کر دے یا اس طریقہ سے کہ کفیل قاضی کی پولیس سے مدد لے کر مکفول بنفسہ کو قاضی کی مجلس میں مکفول لا کے حوالہ کرنے کیلئے پیش کر دے۔
تیسری دلیل . کفالہ بالنفس کو کفالہ بالمال پر قیاس کرنا ہے یعنی جس طرح کفالہ بالمال کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے اسی طرح کفالہ بالنفس کی بھی ضرورت پڑتی ہے اور کفالہ بالنفس میں کفالہ کے معنی ثابت کرنا بھی ممکن ہیں اس طور پر کفیل جس طرح اپنے ذمہ کو مکفول عنہ کے ذمہ کے ساتھ مال کے مطالبہ میں ملا سکتا ہے۔ اسی طرح مکفول نفسہ کو حاضر کرنے کے مطالبہ میں بھی ملا سکتا ہے۔ پس جب کفالہ بالنفس میں کفالہ کے معنی بھی موجود ہیں اور کفالہ بالنفس کی ضرورت بھی پڑتی ہے۔ تو کفالہ بالنفس کے جائز ہونے میں کیا شبہ
رہ جاتا ہے۔
Complete authority is established, and when the surety has authority over his own property, he is also capable of handing it over. And when the surety is capable of handing over property, then kafalah bi al-mal is permissible. Our proof is that the Messenger, the Hashimi (peace be upon him), said: 'Al-za'eem gharim', i.e., the surety is a guarantor. This hadith, due to its generality, indicates the legitimacy of both types of kafalah. The second proof and response to Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is that the claim that the surety is not capable of handing over the person of the guaranteed is false. Rather, the surety is capable of handing over the person of the guaranteed. In that the surety can point out the location of the guaranteed to the person for whom he guaranteed, i.e., the claimant (the one in whose favor the guarantee is made) and the sought (the person guaranteed). Or in this manner: the surety can take assistance from the judge's police and present the guaranteed person to the judge's court for handing over. The third proof is analogy of kafalah bi al-nafs to kafalah bi al-mal. Just as there is a need for kafalah bi al-mal, similarly there is a need for kafalah bi al-nafs. And it is possible to establish the meaning of kafalah in kafalah bi al-nafs: just as the surety can join his own liability to that of the principal in the claim of property, similarly he can join in the claim of presenting the person. So when the meaning of kafalah exists in kafalah bi al-nafs and there is also a need for it, then what doubt remains in the permissibility of kafalah bi al-nafs?
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi proofs for the validity of kafalah bi al-nafs (suretyship for the person). It provides three arguments. First, the hadith 'al-za'eem gharim' (the surety is liable) is general and includes both kafalah bi al-mal and kafalah bi al-nafs. Second, the objection that the surety cannot hand over the person is refuted: the surety can either show the location or use legal authority to produce him. Third, an analogy (qiyas) is drawn: just as kafalah bi al-mal is needed and valid, so is kafalah bi al-nafs, and both involve joining liability. This establishes the permissibility of kafalah bi al-nafs in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contractual surety where a person (kafil) becomes liable for another's debt (kafalah bi al-mal) or for producing the other's person (kafalah bi al-nafs).
زعيم (za'eem): A surety or guarantor, synonymous with kafil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi proofs in this passage, which of the following is NOT a proof for the validity of kafalah bi al-nafs?
A) The hadith 'al-za'eem gharim'
B) The surety's ability to hand over the person by showing his location
C) Analogy (qiyas) to kafalah bi al-mal
D) Consensus (ijma') of the Companions

Key Takeaway
Kafalah bi al-nafs is valid in Hanafi fiqh based on the general hadith, practical possibility of handing over, and analogy with kafalah bi al-mal.

Expressions that Contract Kafalah bi al-Nafs
Listing valid expressions for kafalah bi al-nafs and their rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid and invalid expressions for contracting kafalah bi al-nafs and explain the Hanafi rationale for each.

Core Idea
Any expression that refers to the whole person—literally, customarily, or as an unspecified part—contracts kafalah bi al-nafs.

In Hanafi law, kafalah bi al-nafs can be established by a variety of verbal formulas. The key principle is that the expression must refer to the entire person or an unspecified part, not to a specific limb. This lesson unpacks the valid expressions and their rationale, drawing on analogies from divorce law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core expressions
List the explicit expressions that refer to the whole body: nafs (self), raqabah (neck), ruh (soul), jasad (body), ra's (head), badan (body), wajh (face). These are valid either literally or customarily.
2
Apply the indivisibility principle
Understand that unspecified fractional parts (like half, third, or a part) are valid because the self is indivisible in kafalah. Mentioning an unspecified part is like mentioning the whole.
This is based on the rule that the self cannot be split for purposes of suretyship.
3
Distinguish specific limbs
Recognize that specific limbs like hand (yad) or foot (rijl) are invalid because they do not refer to the whole body, neither literally nor customarily. This is supported by the analogy with divorce where attributing divorce to a specific limb is invalid.
4
Recognize synonymous expressions
Learn that other words like daman (I guarantee), 'alayya (upon me), ilayya (toward me), za'eem (surety), and qabeel (surety) also contract kafalah because they explicitly state the obligation or are customary synonyms.
The hadith: 'Whoever leaves a debt or dependents, they are toward me' supports 'toward me'.
5
Identify invalid synonyms
Note that 'I am a guarantor for his knowledge' (damantu li ma'rifatihi) is invalid because it only guarantees knowledge, not the person's appearance in court.

کن الفاظ سے کفالت منعقد ہوتی ہے
قال: وتنعقد إذا قال تكفلت بنفس فلان او برقبته او بروحه او بجسده او برأسه وكذا ببدنه وبوجهه لان هذه الالفاظ يعبر بها عن البدن اما حقيقة اوعرفا على مامر فى الطلاق وكذا اذا قال بنصفه او بثلثه او بجزء منه لان النفس الواحدة فى حق الكفالة لاتتجزى فكان ذكر بعضها شائعا كذكر كلها بخلاف ما اذا قال تكفلت بید فلان او برجله لانه لا يعبر بهما عن البدن حتى لا يصح اضافة الطلاق اليهما وفيما تقدم يصح ترجمہ اور کفالہ بالنفس منعقد ہو جائے گا جبکہ کفیل نے کہا کہ میں فلاں کے نفس یا فلاں کے رقبہ یا اسکی روح یا اسکے جسم یا اس کے سر کا کفیل ہو گیا اور اسی طرح میں نے اس کے بدن کی کفالت کی یا میں نے اس کے چہرے کی کفالت کی کیونکہ یہ الفاظ ایسے ہیں جن سے پورے بدن کو تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے یا تو حقیقتاً یا عرفاً چنانچہ کتاب الطلاق میں گذر چکا ہے اور اسی طرح اگر کہا کہ (میں نے ) اس کے نصف یا ثلث یا اسکے کسی جز کی کفالت کی کیونکہ کفالت کے حق میں نفس واحدہ کے ٹکڑے نہیں ہوتے ہیں ۔ پس ایک نفس کے جزو شائع کو ذکر کرنا کل نفس کو ذکر کرنے کے مانند ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف اگر کہا کہ میں نے فلاں کے ہاتھ یا اس کے پاؤں کی کفالت کی کیونکہ ان دونوں
سے انسان کے بدن کو تعبیر نہیں کیا جاتا ہے۔ حتی کہ ان کی طرف طلاق کو منسوب کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے اور سابقہ اعضاء میں صحیح ہے۔ تشریح کفالہ کی انواع و اقسام سے فراغت کے بعد یہاں سے ان الفاظ کا ذکر ہے جو الفاظ کفالہ میں مستعمل ہوتے ہیں اور جن الفاظ سے کفالہ منعقد ہو جاتا ہے۔ اس سلسلہ میں ضابطہ یہ ہے کہ جن الفاظ سے انسان کے پورے بدن کو تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے۔ خواہ حقیقت جیسے لفظ

كتاب الكفاله
اشرف الہدایہ جلد - نم نفس، جسد، روح خواہ عرفا جیسے لفظ رقبہ، راس اور وجہ ان سے کفالہ منعقد ہو جائے گا۔ مثلاً کسی نے کہا کہ میں فلاں کے نفس کا یا اس کی گردن کا یا اس کی روح کا یا اس کے جسم کا یا اس کے سرکا یا اس کے بدن کا یا اس کے چہرے کا کفیل ہو گیا تو ان الفاظ سے کفالہ منعقد ہو جائے گا کیونکہ یہ تمام الفاظ ایسے ہیں جن سے حقیقت یا عرفاً پورے بدن کو تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے۔ اور پورے بدن کی کفالت چونکہ صحیح ہے اسلئے مذکور اعضائے بدن کی طرف کفالت کو منسوب کرنے سے کفالہ درست ہو جائے گا۔ جیسا کہ کتاب الطلاق میں گذر چکا کہ اگر مذکورہ اعضائے بدن کی طرف طلاق کو منسوب کیا گیا تو طلاق واقع ہو جاتی ہے۔ پس اسی طرح یہاں بھی مذکورہ اعضائے بدن کی طرف کفالت منسوب کرنے سے کفالہ درست ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر کفالت کو جز و شائع یعنی جز غیر معین کی طرف منسوب کیا گیا مثلا یہ کہا کہ میں فلاں کے نصف يا ثلث یا ایک جز کا کفیل ہو گیا تو یہ کفالت بھی درست ہے۔ کیونکہ ایک نفس کفالہ کے حق میں متجری اور منقسم نہیں ہوتا۔ لہذا جز شائع اور غیر معین جز کوذکر کرنا ایسا ہو گا۔ جیسے کل بدن اور آج کل نفس کو ذکر کرنا۔ ہاں اگر یہ کہا کہ میں فلاں کے ہاتھ یا فلاں کے پاؤں کا کفیل ہو گیا تو کفالہ درست نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ لفظ ہاتھ اور لفظ پاؤں سے انسان کا پورا بدن تعبیر نہیں کیا جاتا۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر ہاتھ یا پاؤں کی طرف طلاق منسوب کی گئی تو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوتی اور جو اعضاء پہلے گذرے ہیں یعنی نفس، رقبہ وغیرہ اور نصف ، ثلث وغیرہ ان کی طرف منسوب کرنے سے طلاق ہو جاتی ہے۔
لفظ ضمنتہ سے بھی کفالت منعقد ہو جاتی ہے
وكذا اذا قال ضمنته لانه تصريح بموجبه او قال هو على لانه صيغة الالتزام اوقال الى لانه في معنى على في هذا المقام قال عليه السلام ومن ترك مالا فلورثته ومن ترک کلا اوعيا لافالى وكذا اذا قال انا زعيم به او قبيل لان الزعامة هى الكفالة وقد روينا فيه والقبيل هو الكفيل ولهذا سمى الصك قبالة بخلاف ما اذا قال انا ضامن لمعرفته لانه التزام المعرفة دون المطالبة
ترجمہ اور اسی طرح اگر کہا کہ میں اس کا ضامن ہو گیا۔ کیونکہ یہ کفالہ کے موجب کی تصریح ہے یا کہا کہ وہ مجھ پر ہے کیونکہ یہ التزام کا صیغہ ہے یا کہا کہ وہ میری طرف ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ اس مقام میں علی کے معنی میں ہے۔ حضور صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا ہے کہ جس شخص نے مال چھوڑا وہ اس کے وارثوں کا ہے اور جس نے کوئی یتیم اولا دیا قابل پرورش قرابت دار چھوڑے تو وہ میری جانب ہیں ۔ اور اسی طرح اگر کہا کہ میں اسکی ضمانت کا زعیم ہوں یا قبیل ہوں کیونکہ زعامت بھی کفالت ہے اور ہم اس بارے میں حدیث روایت کر چکے اور قبیل بھی کفیل ہے۔ اور اسی وجہ سے چک کو قبالہ کہتے ہیں۔ اس کے برخلاف اگر کسی نے کہا کہ میں اس کی شناخت کا ضامن ہوں کیونکہ اس نے شناخت کا التزام کیا
ہے نہ کہ مطالبہ کا۔
تشریح امام ابوالحسن قدوری نے فرمایا کہ لفظ ضمنته ( میں فلاں کے نفس کا ضامن ہو گیا) کہنے سے بھی کفالہ منعقد ہو جاتا ہے کیونکہ لفظ ضمان ادا کرنے سے عقد کفالہ کے موجب کی صراحت ہو گئی ہے اور عقد چونکہ موجب کی تصریح کرنے سے منعقد ہو جاتا ہے اسلئے لفظ ضمان سے کفالہ منعقد ہو جائے گا جیسے عقد بیع لفظ تملیک سے منعقد ہو جاتا ہے۔ کیونکہ تملیک عقد بیع کا موجب ہے اور اگر کہاھو علی یعنی میں نے اپنے اوپر لازم کیا کہ جب اس کی حاضری مطلوب ہوگی تو میں اس کو حاضر کروں گا۔ اس سے بھی کفالہ منعقد ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ لفظ
From which words is kafalah contracted? He said: It is contracted when he says: 'I stand surety for the self of so-and-so', or 'for his neck', or 'for his soul', or 'for his body', or 'for his head', and similarly 'for his body' and 'for his face', because these expressions refer to the whole body, either literally or customarily, as previously in divorce. Similarly when he says: 'for his half', or 'for his third', or 'for a part of him', because the single self with respect to kafalah is not divisible, so mentioning some unspecified part is like mentioning the whole. Contrary to if he says: 'I stand surety for the hand of so-and-so' or 'for his foot', because these do not refer to the whole body, so that adding divorce to them is not valid, but in the previous ones it is valid. Translation: And kafalah bi al-nafs is contracted when the surety says: 'I am surety for the self of so-and-so, or for his neck, or for his soul, or for his body, or for his head, and similarly I guaranteed his body, or I guaranteed his face', because these expressions are used to express the whole body, either literally or customarily, as has passed in the Book of Divorce. And similarly if he said: 'I guaranteed his half, or his third, or some part of him', because with respect to kafalah the single self is not divisible. So mentioning a part that is unspecified is like mentioning the whole. Contrary to if he said: 'I guaranteed the hand of so-and-so' or 'his foot', because these two do not express the whole body, so that attributing divorce to them is not valid, but in the previous organs it is valid. Explanation: After finishing the types and kinds of kafalah, here mention is made of the words used in kafalah and by which words kafalah is contracted. The rule in this regard is that words that express the whole body, whether literally like the word 'self, body, soul' or customarily like the word 'neck, head, and face', from these kafalah will be contracted. For example, if someone says: 'I am surety for the self of so-and-so, or his neck, or his soul, or his body, or his head, or his body, or his face', from these words kafalah will be contracted because all these words express the whole body, literally or customarily. And since kafalah of the whole body is valid, therefore attributing kafalah to the mentioned body parts makes kafalah correct. As has passed in the Book of Divorce that if divorce is attributed to the mentioned body parts, divorce occurs. So similarly here, attributing kafalah to the mentioned body parts makes kafalah correct. And if kafalah is attributed to an unspecified part, i.e., an unspecified part, for example he said: 'I am surety for his half, or third, or one part', then this kafalah is also correct. Because one self is not divisible and partible with respect to kafalah. Therefore mentioning an unspecified part is like mentioning the whole body and the whole self. Yes, if he said: 'I am surety for the hand of so-and-so' or 'the foot of so-and-so', then kafalah will not be correct. Because the word 'hand' and the word 'foot' do not express the whole person. This is the reason that if divorce is attributed to the hand or foot, divorce does not occur. And from the previous organs i.e., self, neck etc., and half, third etc., attributing divorce to them results in divorce. Kafalah is also contracted by the word 'daman'. And similarly if he said: 'I guarantee him', because it is an explicit statement of its consequence. Or he said: 'He is on me', because it is a formula of commitment. Or he said: 'He is toward me', because it is in the meaning of 'on' in this context. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever leaves property, it is for his heirs, and whoever leaves a debt or dependents, then they are toward me.' And similarly if he said: 'I am za'eem for him' or 'qabeel', because za'amah is kafalah, and we have narrated a hadith about it, and al-qabeel is the kafil, and for this reason the document is called qabalah. Contrary to if he said: 'I am a guarantor for his knowledge', because he has committed to knowledge, not to the claim. Translation: And similarly if he said: 'I am his guarantor', because this is an explicit statement of the consequence of kafalah. Or he said: 'He is on me', because it is a formula of commitment. Or he said: 'He is toward me', because it is in the meaning of 'on' in this context. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever leaves property, it is for his heirs, and whoever leaves an orphan or a needy relative, then they are toward me.' And similarly if he said: 'I am za'eem for his guarantee' or 'qabeel', because za'amah is kafalah, and we have narrated a hadith about it, and al-qabeel is the kafil, and for this reason the document is called qabalah. Contrary to if someone said: 'I am a guarantor for his knowledge', because he has committed to knowledge, not to the claim. Explanation: Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Quduri said: By saying the word 'daman' (I became guarantor for the self of so-and-so), kafalah is also contracted, because by uttering the word daman, the consequence of the contract of kafalah is made explicit. And since the contract is contracted by explicit mention of its consequence, therefore kafalah will be contracted by the word daman, just as the contract of sale is contracted by the word tamlik, because tamlik is the consequence of the contract of sale. And if he said 'huwa 'alayya' (he is upon me), meaning I have obligated myself that when his presence is demanded, I will make him present, kafalah will also be contracted because the word...

Commentary
This passage is a definitional ruling on the wording that establishes kafalah bi al-nafs. It begins by stating that kafalah is contracted by expressions like 'takaffaltu bi nafsihi' (I stand surety for his soul) and similar words that refer to the whole body, either literally (like nafs, jasad, ruh) or customarily (like raqabah, raas, wajh). It also covers unspecified parts (like half or third) because the person is indivisible in kafalah. Specific limbs like hand or foot are invalid. Then it extends to synonyms like daman (I guarantee him), 'alayya (upon me), ilayya (toward me), za'eem, and qabeel, citing a hadith for 'toward me'. It distinguishes 'daman for knowledge' as invalid because it only guarantees knowledge, not appearance. Finally, the commentary from Quduri explains that 'daman' is explicit of the consequence (ta'sir), so it contracts kafalah by analogy with sale.

Key Terms
نفس (nafs): A term referring to the whole person or self; used in kafalah to indicate suretyship for the person.
رقبة (raqabah): Literally 'neck'; customarily used to refer to the whole person in kafalah and similar contexts (e.g., manumission).
جزء شائع (juz' sha'i'): An unspecified part or fraction (e.g., half, third) that is not allocated to a specific limb; mentioning it is like mentioning the whole because the self is indivisible in kafalah.
ضمان (daman): A term meaning guarantee or suretyship; when used with the person, it contracts kafalah bi al-nafs because it explicitly states the consequence.
زعيم (za'eem): Surety; used as a synonym for kafil in the hadith 'al-za'eem gharim'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rules in this passage, which of the following expressions would NOT contract a valid kafalah bi al-nafs?
A) 'I am surety for his soul' (takaffaltu bi nafsihi)
B) 'I am surety for his hand' (takaffaltu bi yadihi)
C) 'I am surety for his half' (takaffaltu bi nisfihi)
D) 'I am his guarantor' (damantu)

Key Takeaway
Only expressions that refer to the whole person or an unspecified part, not specific limbs, contract kafalah bi al-nafs.

Kafalah — Surety
beginner
6 min
Valid Expressions for Kafalah bi al-Nafs
Ruling on valid words for kafalah bi al-nafs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given expression constitutes a valid kafalah bi al-nafs by applying the Hanafi rule that the expression must denote the whole person or an undivided share.

Core Idea
Kafalah bi al-nafs requires words denoting the whole person; specific limbs are invalid as they do not represent the entire being.

The validity of a kafalah bi al-nafs contract hinges on the words used to describe the person being guaranteed. The Hanafi school, as presented in this passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah, provides a clear criterion: any expression that by reality or custom refers to the entire person, or an undefined portion of the person, suffices. This lesson will guide you through distinguishing valid from invalid expressions, using the analogy with divorce (talaq) to reinforce the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
Recognize that the passage deals with kafalah bi al-nafs, a guarantee of the person's presence. The wording must refer to the person as a whole.
Kafalah bi al-nafs is distinct from kafalah bi al-mal; here the focus is on the person, not property.
2
Classify expressions as whole or limb
List the provided examples: nafs, raqaba, ruh, jasad, ra's, badan, wajh – these all denote the entire person. Then contrast with hand, foot, etc., which denote only a part.
Custom (urf) may also render some words indicative of the whole, e.g., 'face' often represents the person.
3
Apply the analogy to talaq
The passage cites Kitab al-Talaq: the same expressions that effect divorce are valid for kafalah. This cross-referencing reinforces the rule.
If a word would not cause talaq when attributed to it, it cannot establish kafalah.
4
Understand undivided shares
An expression like 'half' or 'third' of a person is valid because the person is indivisible for kafalah purposes. Mentioning a portion is equivalent to mentioning the whole.
This differs from property guarantees where specific shares are required.
5
State the ruling
Conclude: words denoting the whole person or an unspecified share make kafalah sahih (valid); words denoting a specific limb (hand, foot) make it invalid (ghayr sahih).
The ruling is clear and based on the principle of representation of the entire person.

کتاب الكفاله
اشرف الہدایہ جلد - نم نفس، جسد، روح خواہ عرفا جیسے لفظ رقبہ، راس اور وجہ ان سے کفالہ منعقد ہو جائے گا۔ مثلاً کسی نے کہا کہ میں فلاں کے نفس کا یا اس کی گردن کا یا اس کی روح کا یا اس کے جسم کا یا اس کے سرکا یا اس کے بدن کا یا اس کے چہرے کا کفیل ہو گیا تو ان الفاظ سے کفالہ منعقد ہو جائے گا کیونکہ یہ تمام الفاظ ایسے ہیں جن سے حقیقت یا عرفاً پورے بدن کو تعبیر کیا جاتا ہے۔ اور پورے بدن کی کفالت چونکہ صحیح ہے اسلئے مذکور اعضائے بدن کی طرف کفالت کو منسوب کرنے سے کفالہ درست ہو جائے گا۔ جیسا کہ کتاب الطلاق میں گذر چکا کہ اگر مذکورہ اعضائے بدن کی طرف طلاق کو منسوب کیا گیا تو طلاق واقع ہو جاتی ہے۔ پس اسی طرح یہاں بھی مذکورہ اعضائے بدن کی طرف کفالت منسوب کرنے سے کفالہ درست ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر کفالت کو جز و شائع یعنی جز غیر معین کی طرف منسوب کیا گیا مثلا یہ کہا کہ میں فلاں کے نصف يا ثلث یا ایک جز کا کفیل ہو گیا تو یہ کفالت بھی درست ہے۔ کیونکہ ایک نفس کفالہ کے حق میں متجری اور منقسم نہیں ہوتا۔ لہذا جز شائع اور غیر معین جز کوذکر کرنا ایسا ہو گا۔ جیسے کل بدن اور آج کل نفس کو ذکر کرنا۔ ہاں اگر یہ کہا کہ میں فلاں کے ہاتھ یا فلاں کے پاؤں کا کفیل ہو گیا تو کفالہ درست نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ لفظ ہاتھ اور لفظ پاؤں سے انسان کا پورا بدن تعبیر نہیں کیا جاتا۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر ہاتھ یا پاؤں کی طرف طلاق منسوب کی گئی تو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوتی اور جو اعضاء پہلے گذرے ہیں یعنی نفس، رقبہ وغیرہ اور نصف ، ثلث وغیرہ ان کی طرف منسوب کرنے سے طلاق ہو جاتی ہے۔
Book of Kafalah. Ashraf al-Hidayah volume [nine]. Words like nafs, jasad, ruh, or customarily like raqaba, ra's, and wajh; kafalah is established by them. For example, someone said: I am the kafil of so-and-so's nafs, or his neck, or his soul, or his body, or his head, or his physique, or his face, then kafalah is established by these words. Because all these words indicate the entire body by reality or custom. And since kafalah of the entire body is valid, therefore attributing kafalah to the mentioned body parts makes kafalah valid. As has passed in Kitab al-Talaq that if divorce is attributed to the mentioned body parts, divorce occurs. Similarly here, attributing kafalah to the mentioned body parts makes kafalah valid. And if kafalah is attributed to a shared part (juz' sha'i') i.e., an unspecified part, like one said: I am the kafil of half, or one-third, or one part of so-and-so, then this kafalah is also valid. Because one person is not divisible and separable with respect to kafalah. Therefore mentioning a shared and unspecified part is like mentioning the entire body or the entire person. However, if one said: I am the kafil of so-and-so's hand or foot, then kafalah is not valid. Because the word hand and foot do not indicate the entire person. For this reason, if divorce is attributed to hand or foot, divorce does not occur. And the organs that passed earlier, i.e., nafs, raqaba, etc., and half, one-third, etc., by attributing to them, divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This is a ruling statement on valid expressions for kafalah bi al-nafs. The passage explains that words referring to the entire person, whether by literal meaning or common usage, establish a valid kafalah. Examples include nafs (self), raqaba (neck), ruh (soul), jasad (body), ra's (head), badan (body), wajh (face). These words denote the whole being. Also valid are shares like half, third, or any unspecified portion because the person cannot be divided. However, specific limbs like hand or foot do not represent the whole and thus are invalid. The reasoning is analogized to divorce: same words that effect talaq are valid for kafalah.

Key Terms
نفس (nafs): The whole person; used in kafalah bi al-nafs to refer to the person being guaranteed.
جز شائع (juz' sha'i'): An undivided share or portion (e.g., half, third) of the person, which is valid in kafalah because the person is indivisible.
رقبہ (raqaba): Neck, used metonymically for the whole person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following expressions would make a valid kafalah bi al-nafs according to the text?
A) 'I am the kafil of his hand.'
B) 'I am the kafil of his nafs.'
C) 'I am the kafil of his foot.'
D) 'I am the kafil of his eye.'

Key Takeaway
For kafalah bi al-nafs, the expression must denote the whole person (by reality or custom) or an undivided share; specific limbs are invalid.

Kafalah — Surety
