ہماری طرف سے جواباً عرض ہے کہ کفالہ بالمال فقط بیج کے مشابہ نہیں ہے بلکہ انتہا بیع کے مشابہ ہے۔ بشرطیکہ مکفول عنہ کے حکم سے کفیل ہوا ہو کیونکہ کفیل جب مکفول عنہ سے مال کی وہ مقدار واپس لے گا جو اس نے اس کی طرف سے مکفول لہ کو ادا کی ہے تو یہ مبادلہ المال بالمال ہو کر عقد معاوضہ ہو جائے اور کفالہ بالمال ابتداء نذر کے مشابہ ہے اس طور پر کہ کفیل ابتداء میں تبرعا اپنے اوپر ایک غیر لازم چیز کولازم کرتا ہے۔ جیسا کہ نذر کرنے والا اپنے اوپر ایک غیر لازم چیز کو لازم کرتا ہے۔ بہر حال حاصل یہ ہوا کہ کفالہ بالمال ابتداء منذر کے
مشابہ ہے اور اتنہا ء بیع کے مشابہ ہے اور شرط کی دو قسمیں ہیں۔
ا۔ شرط متعارف جیسے مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر نہ کرنے کی شرط ۲ شرط غیر متعارف جیسے ہوا کا چلنا ، بارش کا برسنا ، مکان میں داخل ہونا۔
واضح ہو کہ بیع کوکسی بھی شرط پر معلق کرنا جائز نہیں ہے نہ شرط متعارف پر اور نہ شرط غیر متعارف پر اور نذر کو دونوں قسم کی شرطوں پر معلق کرنا جائز ہے۔ پس کفالہ بالمال کی بیج کے ساتھ مشابہت کا تقاضہ یہ ہے کہ کفالہ بالمال کو کسی بھی شرط پر معلق کرنا جائز نہ ہو۔ اور نذر کے ساتھ مشابہت کا تقاضہ یہ ہے کہ دونوں قسم کی شرطوں پر معلق کرنا جائز ہو۔ لہذا ہم نے دونوں مشابہتوں پر عمل کرتے ہوئے کہا کہ بیع کی مشابہت کی وجہ سے شرط غیر متعارف پر کفالہ بالمال کو معلق کرنا نا جائز ہے اور نذر کی مشابہت کی وجہ شرط متعارف پر معلق کرنا جائز ہے اور مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر نہ کرنے کی شرط چونکہ شرط متعارف ہے۔ اسلئے کفالہ بالمال کو اس شرط پر معلق کرنا جائز ہے اور جب کفالہ بالمال کو اس شرط پر معلق کرنا جائز ہے تو اس صورت میں کفالہ بالمال جائز اور صحیح ہوگا۔
Book of Kafalah. Volume 9 of Ashraf al-Hidayah. The reason is that the condition may or may not occur. And Kafalah bi al-mal resembles a sale in the obligation of wealth, when it is with the permission of the principal, and the surety takes back from the principal the amount he paid to the creditor. And suspending a sale on a condition is not permissible. For example, if one says, 'If you enter the house, then I have sold this item to you for one hundred rupees,' and the other accepts, then the sale is not established by entering the house. Because sale is a cause of obligation of wealth, and suspending the cause of obligation on a condition, i.e., a contingent matter, is gambling, and gambling is prohibited. Therefore suspending a sale on a condition is also prohibited. And when suspending a sale on a condition is prohibited and impermissible, then that which resembles a sale, i.e., Kafalah bi al-mal, it is also prohibited and impermissible to suspend it on a condition. And in the issue of the text, since the Kafalah bi al-mal is suspended on the condition of not presenting the principal, therefore here too the Kafalah bi al-mal would not be valid. Our response is that Kafalah bi al-mal is not only resembling a sale, but its end resembles a sale, provided that the surety acts with the permission of the principal, because when the surety takes back from the principal the amount he paid to the creditor on his behalf, then this becomes an exchange of wealth for wealth and thus a commutative contract. And Kafalah bi al-mal resembles a vow in the beginning, in that the surety initially makes a non-binding thing binding upon himself voluntarily, just as one who makes a vow makes a non-binding thing binding upon himself. In summary, the result is that Kafalah bi al-mal resembles a vow in the beginning and a sale in the end. And conditions are of two types: 1) Customary condition, such as the condition of not presenting the principal; 2) Non-customary condition, such as the blowing of wind, the falling of rain, entering the house. It is clear that a sale cannot be suspended on any condition, neither customary nor non-customary, and a vow can be suspended on both types of conditions. Therefore, the requirement of the resemblance to a sale is that Kafalah bi al-mal cannot be suspended on any condition, and the requirement of the resemblance to a vow is that it can be suspended on both types. Hence, acting on both resemblances, we say: due to the resemblance to a sale, suspending Kafalah bi al-mal on a non-customary condition is impermissible, and due to the resemblance to a vow, suspending it on a customary condition is permissible. And since the condition of not presenting the principal is a customary condition, therefore suspending Kafalah bi al-mal on this condition is permissible, and when it is permissible, then in this case Kafalah bi al-mal is valid and sound.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary Ashraf al-Hidayah explaining the Hanafi position on attaching conditions to Kafalah bi al-mal (suretyship for debt). The author first presents the objection that Kafalah bi al-mal is like a sale and thus cannot be conditional, then provides the correct Hanafi view. The key insight is that Kafalah bi al-mal has a dual nature: initially it is a gratuitous commitment resembling a vow (nadhr), but in its end it becomes a commutative contract resembling a sale when the surety seeks reimbursement from the principal. Therefore, conditions are distinguished: customary conditions (shart muta'arraf), such as failure to produce the principal, are commonly attached to such commitments and do not introduce uncertainty (gharar), so they are valid. Non-customary conditions (shart ghayr muta'arraf), like weather events, are akin to gambling (qimar) and invalidate the contract if attached. The example given is a condition to present the principal; since it is customary in suretyship, it is valid. This ruling reflects the Hanafi school's nuanced use of istihsan (juristic preference) over strict qiyas, balancing the two analogies to reach a practical conclusion.

Key Terms
کفالہ بالمال (kafalah bi al-mal): Suretyship for debt: a guarantee to pay a financial obligation if the principal fails.
شرط متعارف (shart muta'arraf): A condition that is customary in such contracts, e.g., failure to produce the principal.
شرط غیر متعارف (shart ghayr muta'arraf): A condition that is not customary, e.g., wind blowing or rain falling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, what is the ruling on suspending Kafalah bi al-mal on a customary condition such as 'if I do not bring the principal tomorrow'?
A) It is invalid because sale cannot be conditional.
B) It is valid and permissible because it resembles a vow in the beginning.
C) It is valid only if the principal consents.
D) It is makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, Kafalah bi al-mal can be validly suspended on a customary condition but not on a non-customary one, due to its hybrid nature as both vow and sale.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Death of the Principal and the Surety's Liability for Debt
Ruling with proof, objection, and response on surety's liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a surety's liability when the principal dies, and explain why death does not extinguish the financial obligation.

Core Idea
Death fulfills the condition of non-production, so the surety must pay.

Imagine you guarantee to produce someone, and if you fail, you will pay his debt. Before the deadline, the principal dies. Are you off the hook? The Hanafi school says no—because the condition of failure to produce has been realized by death itself. This ruling rests on a careful distinction between cancellation by mutual release and cancellation by impossibility.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the act (suretyship for person with conditional debt) and the ruling (surety is liable for the debt upon principal's death).
The ruling is wajib (obligatory).
2
Identify the proof
Locate the proof: the condition 'if I do not bring him' is literally satisfied by death, so liability attaches.
Proof is textual from the contract itself.
3
Analyze the objection
Understand the objection: since death cancels the personal suretyship, the dependent financial suretyship should also be cancelled, analogous to release (ibra').
The objection uses qiyas with ibra'.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The response distinguishes ibra' from death. Ibra' is a legal act that annuls entirely; death is an event that only cancels where performance is impossible, not the dependent condition.
Death does not retroactively undo a fulfilled condition.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The surety pays the debt to the creditor and then recovers from the deceased principal's estate if the suretyship was at his request.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle of giving effect to conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن كفل بنفس رجل وقال ان لم يراف به غدا فعليه المال فان مات المكفول عنه ضمن المال لتحقق الشرط وهو عدم الموافاة
ترجمہ اور اگر ایک شخص دوسرے آدمی کا کفیل بانس ہوا اور کہا گر کنول نفسہ کویل آئندہ حاضر نہ کیا تو اس پر مال واجب ہے پس اگر مکفول عند مر گیا تو کفیل مکفول لہ کیلئے مال کا ضامن ہوگا کیونکہ شرط یعنی حاضر نہ کرنا پایا گیا۔
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک شخص کسی کا کفیل بالنفس ہوا اور یہ کہا کہ اگر میں مکفول بنفسہ کو کل آئندہ حاضر نہ کر سکا تو تیرا جو مال مکفول بنفسہ پر ہے اس کو میں ادا کروں گا پھر مکفول عنہ یعنی مکفول بنفسہ مر گیا تو کفیل پر مکفول لا کیلئے مال کا ضمان واجب ہوگا اور پھر اگر یہ کفالہ مکفول بنفسہ کے حکم سے ہو تو کفیل اس مال کو اس کے وارثوں سے لے گا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مال کا ضامن ہونے کی شرط مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر نہ کرنا ہے اور چونکہ یہ شرط پائی گئی اسلئے کفیل پر ضمان واجب ہو جائے گا۔
اشکال یہاں ایک اشکال ہے وہ یہ کہ مسئلہ مذکورہ میں کفالہ بالمال، کفالہ بالنفس پر مرتب ہے اور مکفول بنفسہ کے مرجانے سے کفالہ بالنفس ساقط ہو گیا تو کفالہ بالمال جو اس پر مرتب ہے اس کو بھی باطل ہو جانا چاہئے تھا ۔ جیسا کہ اگر مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر کرنے کی میعاد سے پہلے مکفول لہ نے کفیل کو کفالہ بالنفس سے بری کر دیا تو کفیل، کفالہ بالمال سے بھی بری ہو جاتا ہے۔ پس اسی طرح یہاں بھی جب مکفول بنفسہ کے مرجانے سے کفیل سے کفالہ بالنفس ساقط ہو گیا تو کفالہ بالمال بھی ساقط ہو جانا چاہئے۔
جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ابراء یعنی کفیل کو بری کرنا، کفالہ فسخ کرنے کیلئے موضوع ہے اور موت، کفالہ فسخ کرنے کیلئے موضوع نہیں ہے۔ پس ابراء کی وجہ سے کفالہ من کل وجہ فسخ نہ ہوگا بلکہ بقدر ضرورت فسخ ہو گا ۔ اور ضرورت فقط کفالہ بالنفس کو فسخ کرنے کی ہے نہ کہ کفالہ بالمال کو بائیں طور کہ جب مکفول بنفسہ مر گیا تو کفیل اس کو حاضر کرنے سے عاجز ہو گیا۔ پس اس عجز کی وجہ سے کفالہ بالنفس فسخ اور باطل ہو جائے گا۔ اور کفالہ بالمال کو فسخ کرنے کی عدم ضرورت اسلئے ہے کہ مکفول بنفسہ کے مرجانے سے اس کو حاضر نہ کرنے کی شرط پائی گئی اور جب شرط پائی گئی تو اس کی طرف سے مکفول لہ کو مال ادا کر دے اور پھر اس کے وارثوں سے اس کے مال متروکہ میں سے وصول کر لے۔
Whoever becomes a surety for the person of a man and says, 'If I do not bring him tomorrow, then upon me is the money,' then if the principal dies, he becomes liable for the money because the condition (failure to produce) is realized. Explanation: The issue is: If a person becomes a surety for the person of someone and says, 'If I cannot present the principal tomorrow, then I will pay the money that you have against him,' then the principal dies. The surety becomes liable for the money to the creditor. Then, if this suretyship was with the principal's permission, the surety will take that money from his heirs. The proof is that the condition for liability is failure to present the principal, and since this condition occurred, liability becomes due. Objection: In this issue, Kafalah bi al-mal is dependent on Kafalah bi al-nafs, and with the death of the principal, Kafalah bi al-nafs is canceled, so Kafalah bi al-mal should also become void. For example, if before the deadline the creditor releases the surety from the person suretyship, the surety is released from the money suretyship as well. So here too, since Kafalah bi al-nafs is canceled by death, Kafalah bi al-mal should also be canceled. Response: The answer is that release (ibra') is designed to annul the suretyship, but death is not designed to annul it. So by release, the suretyship is annulled entirely, but by death it is annulled only to the extent necessary. The necessity is only to cancel Kafalah bi al-nafs, not Kafalah bi al-mal. Because when the principal dies, the surety becomes unable to present him; due to this inability, Kafalah bi al-nafs is canceled and invalid. But there is no need to cancel Kafalah bi al-mal, because with the death of the principal, the condition of failure to produce occurred. When the condition occurs, he pays the money to the creditor and then recovers it from the deceased's estate.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents a legal ruling with its proof and then addresses an objection, making it a ruling_with_proof lesson. The ruling is from the Hanafi school: if a surety for the person (kafil bi al-nafs) conditions that he will pay the debt if he fails to produce the principal, and the principal dies, the surety is liable for the debt because the condition (non-production) is fulfilled. The objection argues that since the suretyship for the person is extinguished by death, the dependent suretyship for the debt should also be extinguished, analogous to the case of release (ibra'). The response distinguishes between release (ibra') and death. Release is a legal act intended to annul the contract entirely, so both obligations fall. Death is not a legal act but a fact that makes performance impossible; only the personal obligation (to produce) is annulled due to impossibility, but the condition for the financial obligation (non-production) has already been fulfilled by the death itself. Thus the surety must pay the debt and then recover from the principal's estate if the suretyship was at his request. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that conditions are given effect as long as their realization is possible, and death does not retroactively undo a condition that has been fulfilled. The proof is textual: the condition 'if I do not produce him' is literally satisfied by death. The objection is based on qiyas with ibra', but the Hanafi response uses istihsan based on the nature of the two events.

Key Terms
کفالہ بالنفس (kafalah bi al-nafs): Suretyship for the person: guaranteeing that the principal will be produced.
کفالہ بالمال (kafalah bi al-mal): Suretyship for debt: guaranteeing payment of a financial obligation.
ابراء (ibra'): Release or discharge from an obligation, a legal act that annuls the suretyship.
شرط (shart): Condition upon which an obligation is suspended; here, failure to produce the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a surety for the person who also guarantees payment if he fails to produce the principal, what happens if the principal dies before the deadline?
A) Both suretyships become void, and the surety is free.
B) The suretyship for the person becomes void, but the surety must still pay the debt because the condition of non-production is fulfilled.
C) The surety must pay only if he was negligent.
D) The surety is liable only for the person, not the debt.

Key Takeaway
Death fulfills the condition of non-production, making the surety liable for the debt, and the obligation is not cancelled by the extinction of the personal suretyship.

Kafalah — Surety

Suretyship for an Unspecified Claim: The Shaykhayn vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on suretyship for unspecified claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute regarding suretyship (kafalah) for an unspecified claim and identify the mufta bihi position with its rationale.

Core Idea
A suretyship conditioned on an unspecified debt is valid by custom; the Shaykhayn prevail over Muhammad.

In Hanafi law, the validity of a conditional suretyship (kafalah) for an unspecified debt is contested among the founding imams. This lesson examines the scenario where a surety guarantees to produce the debtor or pay a hundred dinars, without specifying the quality of the dinars. We will explore the arguments of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf versus Muhammad al-Shaybani and understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) and the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
State the Scenario and Legal Question
Identify the exact scenario: A claims 100 dinars from B without specifying quality. C becomes surety for B's person with the condition: if not produced tomorrow, C pays 100 dinars. C fails to produce B. The question is whether the suretyship is valid and C must pay.
The unspecified quality (good or bad) is the key ambiguity.
2
Identify the Hanafi Position (Shaykhayn) and Their Proof
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that C must pay because the money mentioned (al-mal) is definite by context (the debt is known), and custom accepts general claims without immediate specification. Later specification relates back, validating the original suretyship.
They rely on the definite article 'al-' in 'al-mal' to indicate a known debt.
3
Identify Muhammad al-Shaybani's Opposing Position and His Proof
Muhammad argues the suretyship is invalid because an unspecified claim cannot obligate the principal to appear, and without a valid kafalah bi al-nafs, the kafalah bi al-mal (dependent on it) is also invalid. He sees attaching an absolute money to a risk (khatar) as similar to gambling.
Muhammad's objection is that the condition makes the obligation contingent on an unknown, which is impermissible.
4
Analyse the Hanafi Response and the Custom-Based Reasoning
The Shaykhayn respond that the debt is definite in custom; the condition does not introduce excessive uncertainty because the debt is known by context. Further, the claimant's later specification (e.g., stating the dinars are good) relates back to the original claim, clarifying any ambiguity.
This reasoning prioritizes 'urf (custom) and practicality over strict formalism.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The mufta bihi is the view of the Shaykhayn: the surety must pay. The practical difference: under Shaykhayn the surety pays and can seek reimbursement from the principal; under Muhammad the surety pays nothing and the creditor must specify the claim first.
The ruling is more practical for commerce where claims are often made in general terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یک شخص نے دوسرے پر سود بنا کا دعویٰ کیا کھرا اور کھوٹا ہوتا یا نہی تھی کہ اس کا نفیل بنفسہ اس شرط پر ہوا کہ اگر کل حاضر نہ کر سکا تو سو دینا ر لازم ہوں گے، کل حاضر نہ کر سکا تو سو دینا ر لازم ہوں گے
قال ومن ادعي على اخر مائة دينار بينها أولم يبينها حتى تكفل بنفسه رجل على انه ان لم يواف به غداً فعليه المائة عندابي حنيفة وابى يوسف وقال محمد ان لم يبينها حتى تكفل به رجل ثم ادعى بعد ذلك لم يلتفت الى دعواه لانه علق مالاً مطلقاً بخطر الايرى انه لم ينسبه الى ماعليه ولا تصح الكفالة على هذا الوجه وان بينها ولانه لم يصح الدعوى من غير بيان فلا يجب احضار النفس واذالم يجب لا تصح الكفالة بالنفس فلا تصح بالمال لانه بناء عليه بخلاف ما اذا بين ولهما ان المال ذكر معرفا فينصرف الى ما عليه والعادة جرت باجمال في الدعاوى فتصح الدعاوى على اعتبار البيان فاذا بين التحق البيان باصل الدعوى فتبين صحة الكفالة الأولى فيترتب عليها الثانية ترجمہ اگر ایک آدمی نے دوسرے پر ایک سو دینار کا دعویٰ کیا ( اور ) ان کا کھرا کھوٹا ہونا بیان کیا یا بیان نہیں کیا۔ یہاں تک کہ ایک شخص اس کا کفیل بنفسہ ہو گیا این شرط پر کہ اگر اس کو کل آئندہ حاضر نہ کیا تو اس پر ایک سودینار ہیں ، پس وہ کل آئندہ اس کو حاضر نہ کر سکا تو اس پر شیخین کے نزدیک ایک سو دینار ہیں ۔ اور امام محمد نے فرمایا کہ
A person claimed from another a hundred dinars, specifying whether they were good or bad or not specifying, until a man became a surety for his person on the condition that if he does not produce him tomorrow, then a hundred dinars are upon him. Then he did not produce him tomorrow, so according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, a hundred dinars are upon him. And Muhammad said: If he did not specify until a man became his surety, then later claimed, his claim is not considered because he attached an absolute money to a risk. Do you not see that he did not attribute it to what is upon him? And the suretyship is not valid in this way even if he specified? And because the claim is not valid without specification, so producing the person is not obligatory, and when it is not obligatory, the suretyship for the person is not valid, so the suretyship for money is not valid because it is based on it. Unlike if he specified. And for the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): the money is mentioned as definite, so it refers to what is upon him, and custom is to make claims in general terms, so the claim is valid with the intention of specification. So when he specifies later, the specification attaches to the original claim, thus the validity of the first suretyship becomes clear, and the second follows from it. Translation: If a person claimed from another a hundred dinars, specifying whether they were good or bad or not specifying, until a man became a surety for his person on the condition that if he does not produce him tomorrow, then a hundred dinars are upon him, then he did not produce him tomorrow, then according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) a hundred dinars are upon him. And Imam Muhammad said: ... (the text continues with Muhammad's objection).
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (intra_hanafi_dispute) among the founding imams: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf (the Shaykhayn), and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The issue is whether a suretyship (kafalah) combined with a conditional obligation to pay is valid when the original claim is unspecified regarding the quality of the money (e.g., good or bad dinars). The scenario: Person A claims 100 dinars from B without specifying quality. Then C becomes a surety for B's person with the condition: if he does not produce B tomorrow, C will pay 100 dinars. C fails to produce B. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that C must pay the 100 dinars because the claim, though unspecified, is valid in custom; the condition is met. They argue that the money mentioned (al-mal) is definite by context (al-mal al-ma'ruf), and later specification relates back to the original claim, validating the suretyship. Muhammad al-Shaybani argues that the suretyship is invalid from the start because an unspecified claim cannot obligate the principal to be produced, and without a valid suretyship for the person, the suretyship for money (which depends on it) is also invalid. He also notes that attaching an absolute monetary obligation to a risk (like a condition) without specifying the debt is like gambling. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that under the Shaykhayn view, the surety pays and can recover from the principal's estate; under Muhammad's view, the surety does not pay and the creditor must specify his claim first. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school is generally the view of the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) in this matter, as it aligns with custom and practicality.

Key Terms
مطلقاً (mutlaqan): In an absolute or unrestricted manner; here, the surety attached an absolute obligation to a risk without specifying the debt.
خطر (khatar): Risk or contingency; in legal terms, attaching an obligation to a contingent event like a condition.
کفالہ بالنفس (kafalah bi al-nafs): Suretyship for the person; guaranteeing the presence of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), if a surety guarantees to produce a debtor or pay a debt of 100 dinars, and the original claim did not specify whether the dinars were good or bad, what is the ruling when the surety fails to produce the debtor?
A) The surety is not liable because the claim was unspecified.
B) The surety is liable for 100 dinars because the debt is considered definite by context.
C) The surety is liable only if the debtor admits the debt.
D) The suretyship is void from the start.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, following the Shaykhayn, validates a conditional suretyship for an unspecified claim because custom treats the debt as definite, and later specification relates back.

Liability of a Conditional Guarantee After the Debtor's Death
Ruling with proof and objection-response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the effects of death and release (ibra') on a conditional guarantee, and apply the Hanafi ruling when a personal guarantor's principal debtor dies.

Core Idea
Death fulfills the condition of failure to bring, so the money guarantee stands even though the personal guarantee is impossible.

Guarantees in Islamic law often involve conditions that trigger liability. A common scenario is a personal guarantor (kafil bil nafs) who also conditionally guarantees payment if he fails to produce the debtor. What happens when the debtor dies before the deadline? Does death void both guarantees or just the personal one? This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the Hanafi position with a clear objection and response, distinguishing between release and death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a conditional personal guarantee where the guarantor promises to pay the debt if he fails to bring the debtor. The ruling: the guarantor becomes liable for the debt when the debtor dies.
The ruling is based on the occurrence of the condition.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the condition itself: the guarantor said 'if I do not bring him, I will pay'. Death makes it impossible to bring him, fulfilling the condition.
The proof is contractual, not textual (no Quran/hadith).
3
Trace the objection
The objector argues: since death voids the personal guarantee, the dependent money guarantee should also be void, by analogy with release (ibra').
The analogy seems strong at first glance.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The response distinguishes between ibra' (release) and death: ibra' is designed to annul the whole contract, while death only annuls to the extent of necessity. Since the personal guarantee is impossible, it falls; but the money guarantee's condition has been met, so it remains.
Death does not have the same legal effect as a deliberate release.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: the guarantor must pay the debt and may recover from the debtor's estate if the guarantee was by the debtor's order.
If the debtor had ordered the guarantee, the guarantor can reclaim from the estate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتاب الكفاله اشرف الہدایہ جلد نہم تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک شخص کسی کا کفیل بالنفس ہوا اور یہ کہا کہ اگر میں مکفول بنفسہ کو کل آئندہ حاضر نہ کر سکا تو تیرا جو مال مکفول بنفسہ پر ہے اس کو میں ادا کروں گا پھر مکفول عنہ یعنی مکفول بنفسہ مر گیا کہ بنفسہ مرگیا کفیل پر مکفول لا کیلئے مال کا مضمان [ضمان] واجب ہوگا اور پھر اگر یہ کفالہ مکفول بنفسہ کے حکم سے ہو تو کفیل اس مال کو اس کے وارثوں سے لے گا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مال کا ضامن ہونے کی شرط مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر نہ کرنا ہے اور چونکہ یہ شرط پائی گئی اسلئے کفیل پر منمان [ضمان] واجب ہو جائے گا۔ اشکال یہاں ایک اشکال ہے وہ یہ کہ مسئلہ مذکورہ میں کفالہ بالمال، کفالہ بالنفس پر مرتب ہے اور مکفول بنفسہ کے مرجانے سے کفالہ بالنفس ساقط ہو گیا تو کفالہ بالمال جو اس پر مرتب ہے اس کو بھی باطل ہو جانا چاہئے تھا ۔ جیسا کہ اگر مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر کرنے کی میعاد سے لے مکقول لڑ نے کفیل کو کفالہ بالنفس سے بری کر دیا تو کفیل، کفالہ بالمال سے بھی بری ہو جاتا ہے۔ پس اسی طرح یہاں بھی جب مکفول بنفسہ کے مرجانے سے کفیل سے کفالہ بالنفس ساقط ہو گیا تو کفالہ بالمال بھی ساقط ہو جانا چاہئے۔ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ابراء یعنی کفیل کو بری کرنا، کفانہ [کفالہ] مسخ کرنے کیلئے موضوع ہے اور موت، کفالہ مسخ کرنے کیلئے موضوع نہیں ہے۔ پس ابراء کی وجہ سے کفالہ من کل وجہ فسخ نہ ہوگا بلکہ بقدر ضرورت فسخ ہو گا ۔ اور ضرورت فقط کفالہ بالنفس کو فسخ کرنے کی ہے نہ کہ کفالہ بالمال کو بائیں طور کہ جب مکفول بنفسہ مر گیا تو کفیل اس کو حاضر کرنے سے عاجز ہو گیا۔ پس اس عجز کی وجہ سے کفالہ بالنفس فسخ اور باطل ہو جائے گا۔ اور کفالہ بالمال کو فتح [فسخ] کرنے کی عدم ضرورت اسلئے ہے کہ مکفول بنفسہ کے مرجانے سے اس کو حاضر نہ کرنے کی شرط پائی گئی اور جب شرط پائی گئی تو اسکی طرف سے مکفول لا کو مال ادا کر دے اور پھر ا سکے وارثوں سے اسکے مال متروکہ میں سے وصول کر لے۔
The Book of Kafalah. Ashraf al-Hidayah Volume 9. Commentary: The issue is that if a person becomes a guarantor for the person (kafil bil nafs) of another and said: 'If I do not bring the guaranteed person tomorrow, then I will pay the money that is owed by the guaranteed person.' Then the guaranteed person (i.e., the principal debtor) died. On this, the guarantor becomes liable for the money for the guarantee (kafalah bil mal). If this guarantee was by the order of the principal debtor, then the guarantor will take that money from his heirs. The proof is that the condition for being a guarantor for the money is not bringing the principal debtor, and since this condition occurred, the guarantor becomes liable. Objection: Here there is an objection: In the mentioned issue, the guarantee for money (kafalah bil mal) is based on the personal guarantee (kafalah bil nafs), and due to the death of the principal debtor, the personal guarantee is voided, so the guarantee for money which is based on it should also become void. Just as if the creditor released the guarantor from the personal guarantee, the guarantor would be released from the money guarantee as well. Similarly here, when the personal guarantee falls due to death, the money guarantee should also fall. Answer: Its answer is that release (ibra') is established to annul the guarantee, and death is not established to annul the guarantee. So due to release, the guarantee is rescinded completely, but due to death it is rescinded only to the extent of necessity. The necessity is only to rescind the personal guarantee, not the money guarantee. Because when the principal debtor died, the guarantor becomes unable to bring him. So due to this inability, the personal guarantee is rescinded and void. But there is no need to rescind the money guarantee because with the death, the condition of not bringing him occurred, and when the condition occurred, he should pay the money to the creditor on his behalf, and then recover from his heirs from his estate.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and objection-response. It addresses a specific scenario: a person (kafil bil nafs) guarantees to bring the debtor, and conditionally promises to pay the debt if he fails. When the debtor dies, the condition of failure to bring is automatically fulfilled, so the kafil bil nafs becomes liable for the debt according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. The passage then raises an objection: since the personal guarantee is voided by death, shouldn't the conditional money guarantee also be void? The answer distinguishes between release (ibra') and death: release annuls the entire guarantee, but death only rescinds to the extent of necessity—only the personal guarantee becomes impossible, so the money guarantee remains valid because the condition has been met. The guarantor can then recover from the debtor's estate if the guarantee was by the debtor's order.

Key Terms
کفیل بالنفس (kafil bil nafs): A guarantor for the person (surety of appearance) who undertakes to produce the debtor.
کفالہ بالمال (kafalah bil mal): A guarantee to pay a debt if the principal debtor fails to do so.
ابراء (ibra'): Release or discharge from liability, which annuls the guarantee completely.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of a contract or guarantee; here distinguished between complete annulment (ibra') and partial (death).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the condition of failure to bring occurred despite death.
C) Because the guarantor can still bring the debtor.
D) Because the personal guarantee is not affected by death.

Key Takeaway
Death fulfills the condition of failure to bring, so the money guarantee remains despite the impossibility of the personal guarantee; this distinguishes death from release (ibra').

Validity of Kafalah upon Unspecified Debt: Shaykhayn vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah with unspecified debt quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the Shaykhayn and Imam Muhammad regarding the validity of a money guarantee when the debt's quality is unspecified, and state the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
A kafalah bil mal is valid even if the debt's quality is unspecified, because the definite article and custom override uncertainty.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on a subtle point in kafalah: does the guarantor's liability depend on specifying the quality of the debt? The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that it does not—the guarantee is valid from the start. Imam Muhammad disagrees, arguing that an unspecified debt creates uncertainty and invalidates the guarantee. Understanding their proofs is key to grasping how Hanafi jurists balance textual analysis with custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and core question
Read the passage to understand the facts: a claim of 100 dinars without specification, a personal guarantee conditional on production, followed by a money guarantee. The dispute: is the money guarantee valid?
The scenario involves ta'liq (condition) on producing the debtor.
2
State Imam Muhammad's position and proof
Muhammad argues the kafalah is invalid because the debt is mal mutlaq (absolute) attached to a contingent matter (amr mutaraddad) without attribution to the specific debt. He also says that without specification the original claim is invalid, so the personal guarantee is invalid, and the money guarantee follows it.
His argument emphasizes gharar (uncertainty) in the object.
3
State the Shaykhayn's position and proof
The Shaykhayn argue that mentioning the money with the definite article ('al-ma') makes it refer to the known debt owed. They also invoke custom ('urf) that claims can be made summarily, and later specification attaches back to the original claim, validating both guarantees.
Their reasoning uses linguistic analysis and customary practice.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the Shaykhayn, the guarantor must pay the 100 dinars; under Muhammad, he does not. The later Hanafi position (mufta bihi) follows the Shaykhayn.
The tarjih is based on the strength of the Shaykhayn's argument and later jurists' preference.
5
Reflect on how custom ('urf) overrides strict qiyas
This case shows how Hanafi fiqh uses 'urf to validate claims that might otherwise be invalid due to uncertainty. The Shaykhayn prioritize practical custom over a strict analogy.
Classic example of istihsan via 'urf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن ادعي على اخر مائة دينار بينها أولم يبينها حتى تكفل بنفسه رجل على انه ان لم يواف به غداً فعليه المائة عندابي حنيفة وابى يوسف وقال محمد ان لم يبينها حتى تكفل به رجل ثم ادعى بعد ذلك لم يلتفت الى دعواه لانه علق مالاً مطلقاً بخطر الايرى انه لم ينسبه الى ماعليه ولا تصح الكفالة على هذا الوجه وان بينها ولانه لم يصح الدعوى من غير بيان فلا يجب احضار النفس واذالم يجب لا تصح الكفالة بالنفس فلا تصح بالمال لانه بناء عليه بخلاف ما اذا بين ولهما ان المال ذكر معرفا فينصرف الى ما عليه والعادة جرت باجمال في الدعاوى فتصح الدعاوى على اعتبار البيان فاذا بين التحق البيان باصل الدعوى فتبين صحة الكفالة الأولى فيترتب عليها الثانية ترجمہ اگر ایک آدمی نے دوسرے پر ایک سو دینار کا دعویٰ کیا ( اور ) ان کا کھرا کھوٹا ہونا بیان کیا یا بیان نہیں کیا۔ یہاں تک کہ ایک شخص اس کا کفیل بنفسہ ہو گیا این شرط پر کہ اگر اس کو کل آئندہ حاضر نہ کیا تو اس پر ایک سودینار ہیں ، پس وہ کل آئندہ اس کو حاضر نہ کر سکا تو اس پر شیخین کے نزدیک ایک سو دینار ہیں ۔ اور امام محمد نے فرمایا کہ اگر اس کو بیان نہیں کیا یہاں تک کہ ایک آدمی اس کا کفیل ہو گیا۔ پھر کفالہ کے بعد دعویٰ کیا تو اس کے دعوی کی طرف توجہ نہ کی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ اس نے مال مطلق کو ایک امر متردد پر معلق کیا ہے۔ کیا نہیں دیکھتے ہو کہ کفیل نے ان دیناروں کو اس کی طرف منسوب نہیں کیا امر جو مکفول عنہ پر ہیں اور اس طریقہ پر کفالہ درست نہیں ہوتا۔ اگر چہ دیناروں کی صفت بیان کر دے اور اسلئے کہ بغیر بیان صفت کے دعوئی صحیح نہیں ہے۔ لہذا مکفول بنفسہ کا حاضر کرنا واجب نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب حاضر کرنا واجب نہیں تو کفالہ بالنفس بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا اور کفالہ بالمال بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ کفالہ بالمال ، کفالہ بانس پر مبنی ہے۔ برخلاف اسکے جب مال کی صفت بیان کر دے اور شیخین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مال کو اس نے معرفہ ذکر کیا ہے۔ لہذا یہ اسی کی طرف لوٹے گا جو مکفول عنہ پر واجب ہے دعاوی میں اجمالی بیان کی عادت جاری ہے تو بیان کا اعتبار کر کے مجمل دعوی صحیح ہو جاتا ہے۔ پس جب اس نے بیان کیا تو اصل دعوی کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جائے گا۔ پھر کفالہ اولی کا صحیح ہونا ظاہر ہو گیا تو اس پر کفالہ ثانیہ بھی مرتب ہو جائے گا۔
He (al-Quduri) said: And if someone claims one hundred dinars from another, whether he specifies their quality or not, until a man becomes a personal guarantor for him on condition that if he does not produce him tomorrow, then the hundred are upon him — according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if he does not produce him tomorrow, the hundred are upon him. And Muhammad said: If he did not specify them, until a man became his guarantor, then he (the claimant) made a claim after that, his claim will not be heeded because he attached an absolute debt to a contingent matter. Do you not see that he did not attribute them to what is owed? And the kafalah is not valid in this way, even if he specifies (later). And because without specification, the claim is not valid, so bringing the principal debtor is not obligatory. And when it is not obligatory, the personal guarantee is not valid, and the money guarantee is not valid because it is based on it. Contrary to when he specifies. And the proof of the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that he mentioned the money as definite (ma'rifah), so it refers to what is owed, and custom runs with summary in claims, so the claim is valid on the basis of specification. So when he specifies, the specification attaches to the original claim, and the validity of the first guarantee becomes clear, and the second (money guarantee) follows from it. Translation: If a man claimed one hundred dinars from another, whether he specified their quality or not, until a man became his personal guarantor on condition that if he does not produce him tomorrow, then the hundred are upon him. Then he could not produce him the next day, so according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the hundred are upon him. And Imam Muhammad said: If he did not specify them until a man became his guarantor, then after the kafalah he made a claim, his claim will not be considered. Because he attached an absolute money to a contingent matter. Do you not see that the guarantor did not attribute these dinars to what is owed by the principal debtor? And kafalah in this manner is not valid, even if he specifies the quality of the dinars. And because without specification the claim is not valid, so bringing the principal debtor is not obligatory. And when it is not obligatory, the personal guarantee is not valid, and the money guarantee is not valid, because the money guarantee is based on the personal guarantee. Contrary to when he specifies the money's quality. And the proof of the two shaykhs is that he mentioned the money as definite, so it refers to what is owed on the principal debtor. Custom has been established for summary (ijmal) in claims, so the claim becomes valid on the basis of specification. So when he specifies, it attaches to the original claim, and thus the validity of the first (personal) guarantee becomes clear, and the second (money) guarantee follows from it.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad regarding the validity of a kafalah bil mal when the debt's quality (sound or defective) is unspecified. The scenario: a creditor claims 100 dinars but does not specify their quality; a third party becomes a personal guarantor (kafil bil nafs) on condition that if he fails to produce the debtor, he will pay the 100. Imam Muhammad argues that such a kafalah is invalid because the debt is absolute (mal mutlaq) and not attributed to the specific debt owed, creating uncertainty (gharar) akin to bribery. He further argues that without specification, the original claim itself is invalid, so the personal guarantee cannot be valid, and consequently the money guarantee fails. The Shaykhayn counter: the debt is mentioned with the definite article ('al-ma'), so it automatically refers to the known debt of the principal debtor. Custom in legal claims allows summary mention, and later specification attaches to the original claim, validating both guarantees. Thus, according to the Shaykhayn, the kafalah is valid from the outset.

Key Terms
مال مطلق (mal mutlaq): An absolute, unspecified debt without qualification of quality (sound/deficient).
امر متردد (amr mutaraddad): A contingent matter that may or may not happen (here, non-production of debtor).
تعلیق (ta'liq): Suspending a liability on a future condition.
عرف ('urf): Customary practice in legal claims, allowing summary mention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Muhammad consider the kafalah invalid when the debt's quality is unspecified?
A) Because the debtor must be present to confirm the debt.
B) Because the guarantor attached an absolute debt to a contingent matter without attribution.
C) Because the creditor must first prove the debt in court.
D) Because the guarantee cannot be conditional.

Key Takeaway
The Shaykhayn's view, that a kafalah bil mal is valid even without specifying the debt's quality because custom allows summary mention, is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Kafalah with Unspecified Property
Dispute between Imam Muhammad and the Shaykhayn on validity of kafalah when property amount is known but quality is unspecified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Muhammad held the kafalah invalid while the Shaykhayn held it valid, and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
A kafalah is invalid if the property is absolute and not tied to the debt — but the definite article can cure it.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a kafalah (guarantee) is valid when the creditor initially does not describe the quality of the debt property. Imam Muhammad argues that such a guarantee is invalid due to uncertainty and the risk of bribery, while the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) maintain that it is valid if the property is specified by a definite article, and later description cures any defect. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify when a kafalah is enforceable and when it is not, especially in real-life financial guarantees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
Understand the scenario: a creditor claims a debt of 100 dinars from a debtor without describing the quality (good, bad, mediocre). A guarantor then says: 'If I don't bring the debtor tomorrow, I owe you the 100 dinars.' The guarantor does not explicitly link the 100 dinars to the existing debt.
The key is that the property (100 dinars) is mentioned with the definite article 'al-mi'ah'.
2
State Imam Muhammad's position and proofs
Imam Muhammad considers the kafalah invalid. His first proof: the property is absolute (mal mutlaq) and suspended on a contingent condition, creating a possibility of bribery. Second proof: the original claim is invalid without quality description, so the kafalah for the person is invalid, and consequently the kafalah for property (which is based on it) is also invalid.
Imam Muhammad's reasoning emphasizes the need to eliminate ambiguity and bribery risk.
3
State the Shaykhayn's position and proofs
The Shaykhayn hold the kafalah valid. First proof: the definite article 'al' in 'al-mi'ah' specifies the debt, removing the bribery possibility. Second proof: by custom, summary claims are allowed outside court; later description attaches to the original claim and validates it.
The Shaykhayn rely on linguistic specification and customary practice.
4
Determine the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of the Shaykhayn: the kafalah is valid. This is recorded in the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Imam Muhammad's view is a minority but legitimate intra-school opinion.

كتاب الكفاله
اگر اس کو بیان نہیں کیا یہاں تک کہ ایک آدمی اس کا کفیل ہو گیا۔ پھر کفالہ کے بعد دعویٰ کیا تو اس کے دعوی کی طرف توجہ نہ کی جائے گی۔ کیونکہ اس نے مال مطلق کو ایک امر متردد پر معلق کیا ہے۔ کیا نہیں دیکھتے ہو کہ کفیل نے ان دیناروں کو اس کی طرف منسوب نہیں کیا امر جو مکفول عنہ پر ہیں اور اس طریقہ پر کفالہ درست نہیں ہوتا۔ اگر چہ دیناروں کی صفت بیان کر دے اور اسلئے کہ بغیر بیان صفت کے دعوئی صحیح نہیں ہے۔ لہذا مکفول بنفسہ کا حاضر کرنا واجب نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب حاضر کرنا واجب نہیں تو کفالہ بالنفس بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا اور کفالہ بالمال بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ کفالہ بالمال ، کفالہ بانفس پر مبنی ہے۔ برخلاف اسکے جب مال کی صفت بیان کر دے اور شیخین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مال کو اس نے معرفہ ذکر کیا ہے۔ لہذا یہ اسی کی طرف لوٹے گا جو مکفول عنہ پر واجب ہے دعاوی میں اجمالی بیان کی عادت جاری ہے تو بیان کا اعتبار کر کے مجمل دعوی صحیح ہو جاتا ہے۔ پس جب اس نے بیان کیا تو اصل دعوی کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جائے گا۔ پھر کفالہ اولی کا صحیح ہونا ظاہر ہو گیا تو اس پر کفالہ ثانیہ بھی مرتب ہو جائے گا۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص مثلا خالد نے دوسرے شخص مثلا حامد پر ایک سو دینار کا دعویٰ کیا اور مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ خالد نے ان دینار کی صفت یعنی ان کا جید ہونا یا ردی ہونا یا متوسط ہونا بیان کیا ہو یا بیان نہ کیا ہو دونوں صورتوں میں اگر کوئی تیسرا آدمی مثلاً واصف، قرضدار کی طرف سے قرض خواہ کیلئے کفیل بنفسہ ہو گیا اور یہ کہا کہ میں کل آئندہ قرضدار کو حاضر کر دوں گا۔ اگر کل آئندہ میں قرضدار یعنی حامد کو حاضر مجلس نہ کر سکا تو مجھے پر ایک سو دینار ہیں۔ یعنی ان کو ادا کرنے کا ذمہ دار میں ہوں لیکن کل آئندہ میں کفیل بنفسہ یعنی واصف، مکفول نفسہ یعنی حامد کو حاضر نہ کر سکا تو شیخین کے نزدیک شرط کے مطابق کفیل یعنی واصف پر ایک سو دینار لازم ہوں گے۔ اس کے قائل امام ابو حنیفہ ہیں ۔ اور امام محمد نے فرمایا کہ اگر مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ خالد نے دینار کی صفت یعنی جید ہونا یا ردی ہونا بیان نہیں کیا۔ یہاں تک کہ ایک آدمی یعنی واصف قرضدار یعنی حامد کو حاضر کرنے کا کفیل ہو گیا۔ پھر کفالہ کے بعد مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ خالد نے ان دینار کی صفت یعنی جید یار دی ہونا بیان کی تو اسکے دعوئی کی طرف التفات نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ اس کے قائل امام شافعی ہیں ۔ حاصل یہ کہ شیخین کے نزدیک دنانیر کی مقدار معلوم ہونے کے بعد کفالہ صحیح ہے اگر چہ ان کی صفت مجہول ہے اور امام محمد کے نزدیک جب صفت مجہول ہو تو مکفول لیہ کو کفیل سے مطالبہ کاحق نہیں ہوتا۔ اور جب دعوئی مجہول ہوا تو دعوی ہی باطل ہے۔
امام محمد کی پہلی دلیل یہ ہے کہ کفیل نے جس مال کو مکفول بنفسہ کے حاضر نہ کرنے پر معلق کیا ہے وہ مال مطلق ہے کیونکہ کفیل نے ایک سو کو ان دنانیر کی طرف منسوب نہیں کیا جو مکفول عنہ یعنی قرضدار پر واجب ہیں۔ چنانچہ مکفول لہ یعنی قرض خواہ کو مخاطب بنا کر یہ نہیں کہا کہ اگر میں مکفول بنفسہ یعنی قرضدار کو کل آئندہ حاضر نہ کر سکا تو مجھے پر وہ سو دینار واجب ہوں گے جو تیرے قرضدار یعنی مکفول بنفسہ پر واجب ہیں اور جس چیز پر معلق کیا ہے وہ امر متردد ہے یعنی ایسا امر ہے جو ہو بھی سکتا ہے اور نہیں بھی ہوسکتا۔ کیونکہ شرط مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر نہ کرنا ہے۔ پس یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ کفیل مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر نہ کرے اور یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ حاضر کر دے۔ حاصل یہ ہوا کہ کفیل نے اپنے اس کفالہ میں مال مطلق کو امر متردد پر معلق کیا ہے اور اس طریقہ پر کفالہ صحیح نہیں ہوتا اگر چہ مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ نے دینار کی صفت جید ہونا یا ردی ہونا بھی بیان کر دیا ہو۔ اور اس طریقہ پر کفالہ اسلئے باطل نہیں ہوتا کہ اس میں رشوت کا احتمال ہے۔ کیونکہ جب ایک سو کو ان دینار کی طرف منسوب نہیں کیا جو مکفول بنفسہ یعنی قرضدار پر واجب ہیں تو اس میں جہاں یہ احتمال ہے کہ کفیل نے اپنے اوپر ان دینار کو لازم کیا ہے جو مکفول بنفسہ یعنی قرضدار پر واجب ہیں۔ وہیں یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ کفیل نے ابتداء بطور رشوت کے اپنے اوپر ایک سو دینار کا التزام اسلئے کیا ہے تا کہ مکفول لہ مکفول بنفسہ یعنی قرضدار سے فی الحال مطالبہ کو چھوڑ دے۔ یعنی کفیل نے بطور رشوت مکفول الہ کو ایک سو دینار دیئے تاکہ مکفول لہ یعنی قرض خواہ مکفول بنفسہ یعنی قرضدار کو ایک مدت تک مہلت دے دے اور رشوت کے طور پر چونکہ اپنے اوپر مال لازم کرنا صحیح نہ ہوا تو یہ کفالہ بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا۔ اس کو یوں بھی کہا جاسکتا ہے کہ کفیل نے اپنے اوپر جو مال مطلق لازم کیا ہے وہ بطور رشوت بھی ہو سکتا ہے اور وہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے جو مال مکفول بنفسہ یعنی قرضدار پر واجب تھا۔ پہلی صورت یعنی رشوت کی صورت میں مال لازم نہیں ہوتا اور دوسری صورت یعنی ضامن ہونے کی صورت میں مال لازم ہو جاتا ہے پس کفیل پر مال لازم ہونے میں شک ہو گیا۔ درآنحالیکہ کفیل پر پہلے سے مال لازم نہیں تھا۔ اور شک کی وجہ سے غیر لازم مال چونکہ لازم نہیں ہوتا۔ اسلئے یہاں بھی کفیل پر مال لازم نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب کفیل پر مال لازم نہیں ہوا تو کفالہ بالمال بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا۔
دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ مسئلہ مذکورہ میں کفالہ بالنفس ہی باطل ہے کیونکہ کفالہ بانفس کا صحیح ہونا اس پر موقوف ہے کہ مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ کا دعوی قرض صحیح ہو اور قرض کا دعوی اس وقت صحیح ہوتا ہے جبکہ قرض کے مال کی مقدار بھی معلوم ہو اور اس کی صفت یعنی جید یا ردی ہونا بھی معلوم ہو اور اس جگہ مسئلہ یہ فرض کیا گیا ہے کہ مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ نے مال قرض کا وصف جید یا ردی ہونا بیان نہیں کیا اور جب وصف بیان نہیں کیا تو بغیر بیان وصف کے مدعی مجہول ہو گیا اور مدعی مجہول ہونے کی صورت میں دعوی صحیح نہیں ہوتا لہذا قرض خواہ کا دعوی قرض صحیح نہ ہوا اور جب قرض خواہ دعوی صحیح نہ ہوا تو کفیل پر قرضدار یعنی مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر کرنا بھی واجب نہ ہوا اور جب کفیل پر قرضدار یعنی مکفول بانفسہ کو حاضر کرنا واجب نہ ہوا تو کفالہ بالنفس بھی صحیح نہ ہوا اور جب کفالہ بالنفس صحیح نہیں ہے تو کفالہ بالمال بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ کفالہ بالمال، اس مسئلہ میں کفالہ بالنفس پر مبنی ہے پس جب بنی علیہ ہی صحیح نہیں ہے تو بینی کس طرح صحیح ہوگا۔ اس کے برخلاف اگر قرض خواہ نے قرض کا دعوی کرتے وقت مدعی بہ یعنی مال قرض کا وصف بھی بیان کر دیا ہو تو کفالہ بالنفس صحیح ہو جائے گا۔ اور جب کفالہ بالنفس صحیح ہے تو کفالہ بالمال جو اس پر مبنی ہے وہ بھی درست ہو جائے گا۔ شیخین کی دلیل جو امام محمد کی پہلی دلیل کا جواب بھی ہے یہ ہے کہ کفیل کے قول على انه ان لم يواف به غداً فعليه المائة میں المائة یعنی ایک سو دینار معرف باللام ہے اور لام بھی عہد خارجی کا ہے۔ جس کا مدخول معین ہوتا ہے۔ پس اس المائۃ سے مراد وہ سو دینار ہیں جو مکفول بنفسہ یعنی قرضدار پر واجب ہیں۔ اور جب المائہ سے مراد وہی سو دینار ہیں تو یہ سو دینار رشوت ہونے سے خارج ہو گئے اور جب رشوت کا احتمال نہ رہا تو یہ مال کفالہ کی وجہ سے کفیل پر لازم ہوگا اور جب کفیل پر لازم ہو گیا تو یہ کفالہ بھی درست جائے گا۔ شیخین کی دوسری دلیل جو امام محمد کی دوسری دلیل کا جواب بھی ہے یہ ہے کہ قرض خواہ نے جب قرضدار پر ایک سو دینار کا دعویٰ کیا اور اس کا وصف یعنی جید یا ردی ہونا بیان نہیں کیا یہاں تک کہ تیسرا آدمی قرضدار کی طرف سے کفیل بنفسہ ہو گیا پھر مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ نے کفالہ کے بعد مال کا وصف بیان کیا تو اس میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے کیونکہ لوگوں کی عادت یہ ہے کہ وہ مجلس قاضی کے علاوہ میں مدعی علیہ کے حیلوں کو دور کرنے کے لئے دعاوی کو مجمل رکھتے ہیں اور مجلس قاضی میں جب ضرورت پڑتی ہے تو اوصاف وغیرہ بیان کر کے اس کے تمام گوشوں کو اجاگر کرتے ہیں۔ پس بیان اوصاف پر اعتماد کر کے مجمل دعوی صحیح ہو جاتا ہے۔ پھر جب مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ نے ایک سو دینار مال کا وصف بیان کر دیا تو یہ بیان وصف اصل دعوی کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جاتا ہے۔ یعنی ایسا ہو جاتا ہے گویا اس نے دعوی کرتے
Book of Kafalah. If he did not describe it, until a person became his guarantor, then after the suretyship he made a claim, his claim will not be considered. Because he suspended absolute property on a contingent matter. Do you not see that the guarantor did not attribute those dinars to the obligation that is upon the guaranteed person? In this manner, the suretyship is not valid, even if he describes the quality of the dinars, because without describing the quality, the claim is not valid. Therefore, bringing the guaranteed person is not obligatory. And when bringing is not obligatory, the suretyship for the person is not valid, and the suretyship for property is also not valid, because the suretyship for property is based on the suretyship for the person. Unlike when he describes the quality of the property. And the proof of the Shaykhayn is that he mentioned the property with the definite article. Therefore, it refers back to what is obligatory upon the guaranteed person. In claims, the habit of summary description is prevalent, so the summary claim becomes valid by relying on the description. Thus, when he described, it attaches to the original claim. Then the validity of the first suretyship becomes clear, and the second suretyship is also established upon it. Explanation of the case: A person, e.g., Khalid, claimed one hundred dinars from another person, e.g., Hamid, and the claimant, the creditor Khalid, either described the quality of those dinars—good, bad, or mediocre—or did not describe. In both situations, if a third person, e.g., Wasif, becomes a guarantor for the debtor on behalf of the creditor, saying: 'I will bring the debtor tomorrow. If tomorrow I do not bring the debtor, Hamid, to the court, then upon me are one hundred dinars,' i.e., I am responsible for paying them. But if tomorrow the guarantor for the person, Wasif, does not bring the guaranteed person, Hamid, then according to the Shaykhayn, the condition applies and the guarantor, Wasif, must pay one hundred dinars. Imam Abu Hanifa holds this view. Imam Muhammad said: If the claimant, creditor Khalid, did not describe the quality of the dinars—good or bad—until a person, Wasif, became the guarantor for bringing the debtor Hamid, then after the suretyship, the claimant, creditor Khalid, described the quality of those dinars—good or bad—his claim will not be considered. Imam Shafi'i holds this view. The conclusion: According to the Shaykhayn, once the quantity of the dinars is known, the suretyship is valid even if their quality is unknown. According to Imam Muhammad, when the quality is unknown, the guaranteed party has no right to demand from the guarantor, and since the claim is unknown, the claim itself is invalid. Imam Muhammad's first proof: The property that the guarantor suspended on the failure to bring the guaranteed person is absolute property, because the guarantor did not attribute the one hundred to those dinars that are obligatory upon the guaranteed person, i.e., the debtor. Thus, he did not address the guaranteed party, the creditor, saying: 'If I do not bring the guaranteed person, the debtor, tomorrow, then upon me are those one hundred dinars that are obligatory upon your debtor, the guaranteed person.' And the matter on which it is suspended is a contingent matter, i.e., something that may or may not happen. Because the condition is not bringing the guaranteed person. So it is possible that the guarantor does not bring the guaranteed person, and it is also possible that he does bring him. The conclusion is that the guarantor, in this suretyship, suspended absolute property on a contingent matter, and in this manner the suretyship is not valid, even if the claimant, the creditor, described the quality of the dinars—good or bad. And this suretyship is not invalid because there is a possibility of bribery. Because when the one hundred are not attributed to those dinars that are obligatory upon the guaranteed person, the debtor, then on one hand there is the possibility that the guarantor obligated upon himself those dinars that are obligatory upon the guaranteed person, the debtor; on the other hand, there is also the possibility that the guarantor initially obligated upon himself one hundred dinars as a bribe so that the guaranteed party, the creditor, would abandon his immediate demand from the guaranteed person, the debtor. That is, the guarantor gave one hundred dinars as a bribe to the guaranteed party so that the creditor would give the debtor a period of time. And since obligating property upon oneself as a bribe is not valid, this suretyship is also not valid. It can also be said that the absolute property that the guarantor obligated upon himself could be a bribe, or it could be the property that was obligatory upon the guaranteed person, the debtor. In the first case, i.e., bribery, the property does not become obligatory; in the second case, i.e., being a guarantor, the property becomes obligatory. Thus, there is doubt about whether the property becomes obligatory upon the guarantor, whereas before it was not obligatory. And because of doubt, property that is not obligatory does not become obligatory. Therefore, here also the property does not become obligatory upon the guarantor. And when the property does not become obligatory upon the guarantor, the suretyship for property is also not valid. Second proof: In this case, the suretyship for the person is itself invalid, because the validity of the suretyship for the person depends on the validity of the creditor's claim for the debt. The claim for the debt is valid only when the quantity of the debt property is known and its quality—good or bad—is also known. And in this case, the scenario is that the claimant, the creditor, did not describe the quality of the debt property—good or bad. When he did not describe, without description the claim becomes unknown, and when the claim is unknown, it is not valid. Therefore, the creditor's claim for the debt was not valid. And when the creditor's claim was not valid, then bringing the debtor, i.e., the guaranteed person, is not obligatory upon the guarantor. And when bringing the debtor, the guaranteed person, is not obligatory upon the guarantor, the suretyship for the person is not valid. And when the suretyship for the person is not valid, the suretyship for property is also not valid, because in this case the suretyship for property is based on the suretyship for the person. So when the foundation itself is not valid, how can the building be valid? In contrast, if the creditor, when making the claim for the debt, also described the quality of the claimed property, i.e., the debt property, then the suretyship for the person becomes valid. And when the suretyship for the person is valid, the suretyship for property, which is based on it, is also valid. The proof of the Shaykhayn, which is also a response to Imam Muhammad's first proof, is that the guarantor's statement 'upon me that if he does not come tomorrow, then upon him are the one hundred' — the 'one hundred' is definite with the definite article 'al', and this 'al' is for external reference, whose referent is specific. Therefore, by 'al-mi'ah' is meant those one hundred dinars that are obligatory upon the guaranteed person, the debtor. And when 'al-mi'ah' refers to those one hundred dinars, then these one hundred dinars are excluded from being a bribe. And when there is no possibility of bribery, this property becomes obligatory upon the guarantor due to the suretyship. And when it becomes obligatory upon the guarantor, this suretyship is valid. The second proof of the Shaykhayn, which is also a response to Imam Muhammad's second proof, is that when the creditor claimed one hundred dinars from the debtor and did not describe its quality—good or bad—until a third person became a guarantor for the debtor, then the claimant, the creditor, described the quality of the property after the suretyship. There is no harm in this, because people have the habit of keeping claims summary outside the court to avoid schemes of the defendant, and when needed in court, they describe the qualities and clarify all aspects. So the summary claim becomes valid by relying on the description. Then when the claimant, the creditor, described the quality of the one hundred dinars property, this description attaches to the original claim, i.e., it becomes as if he described it when making the claim.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a specific type of suretyship (kafalah) where the guarantor (kafil) promises to bring the debtor (makful bihi) or else pay a sum of money. The dispute revolves around whether the money is tied to the debt or could be a bribe. The passage begins by stating the issue: if a guarantor guarantees to bring the debtor, and the creditor later describes the property's quality, the claim is not accepted according to Imam Muhammad, because the property was absolute and not attributed to the debt. Imam Muhammad gives two proofs: first, that the property is absolute and suspended on a contingent matter, which creates a possibility of bribery; second, that the suretyship for the person is invalid because the original claim was invalid due to lack of description. The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) respond: first, the definite article 'al' in 'al-mi'ah' specifies the debt, removing the bribery possibility; second, the custom of keeping claims summary outside court allows later description to attach to the original claim, making it valid. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Shaykhayn, as this is the view in the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of guarantee or suretyship where a person (kafil) becomes responsible for another's person or debt.
كفيل (kafil): The guarantor who undertakes the obligation to bring the debtor (in kafalah bi al-nafs) or pay the debt (in kafalah bi al-mal).
مكفول به (makful bihi): The person or property that is the subject of the guarantee. In this text, it is the debtor (person) and the debt (property).
مال مطلق (mal mutlaq): Absolute property without any specification to a particular debt or obligation.
معرفہ (mu'arrafah): The definite article 'al' used to specify a particular known entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what makes the kafalah valid even if the creditor initially does not describe the quality of the debt?
A) The guarantor's acceptance of liability
B) The definite article 'al' in 'al-mi'ah' specifying the debt
C) The creditor's later description of quality
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi position is that a kafalah is valid if the property is specified by a definite article, even without initial quality description, because later description cures any ambiguity.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Validity of Kafalah bi al-Mal with Bribery and Incomplete Claim
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah validity when money is bribe or claim unspecified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on kafalah bi al-mal when the surety gives a bribe and when the creditor's claim lacks description, and evaluate the proofs of Imam Muhammad and the Shaykhayn.

Core Idea
A definite article and later description can cure doubts about the object of suretyship.

This passage presents a dispute between the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad regarding the validity of a suretyship for debt when the surety gives money to the creditor as a bribe to obtain a delay, and when the creditor's claim initially lacked a description of the debt's quality. Imam Muhammad argues invalidity due to doubt and incomplete claim; the Shaykhayn uphold validity through linguistic analysis of the definite article and the custom of summary claims later detailed in court. The commentator declares Imam Muhammad's view incorrect, making the Shaykhayn's position the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the dispute
Read the passage to understand the facts: a surety (kafil) gives a hundred dinars to the creditor (makful lahu) as a bribe to grant a delay to the debtor. The surety then says 'If he does not bring him tomorrow, upon me is the hundred.' Is the surety liable? Also, the creditor's initial claim lacked description of the debt's quality (good/bad). Imam Muhammad says the kafalah is invalid; the Shaykhayn say it is valid. Note the two separate arguments.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi, so both sides are within the same madhhab.
2
Analyze Imam Muhammad's first argument (bribery doubt)
Imam Muhammad argues that the money could be a bribe (no obligation) or the debt (obligation), creating doubt (shakk). Since the surety had no prior obligation, doubt prevents obligation, so the wealth is not obligatory and the kafalah bi al-mal fails.
This is based on the principle that doubt in the cause prevents obligation.
3
Analyze the Shaykhayn's response (definite article)
The Shaykhayn respond by pointing to the definite article 'al-' in 'al-mi'ah' (the hundred). The lam is for external specification (uhud khariji), meaning it refers to the specific known debt, not a bribe. Thus the bribe possibility is removed, and the surety's obligation is clear, making the kafalah valid.
This is a linguistic argument rooted in Arabic grammar and Hanafi usul.
4
Analyze Imam Muhammad's second argument (incomplete claim) and Shaykhayn's response
Imam Muhammad argues that the creditor's claim initially lacked description of quality, making the claimed object (mudda'a bihi) unknown (majhul). An unknown claim is invalid, so the kafalah bi al-nafs is invalid, and since kafalah bi al-mal depends on it, it too is invalid. The Shaykhayn respond that it is customary (urf) for people to give a summary claim outside court and detail it in court; the later description attaches retroactively to the original claim, perfecting it. Thus the claim becomes valid, the kafalah bi al-nafs becomes valid, and consequently the kafalah bi al-mal is valid.
The principle at play: 'al-wasfu al-muta'akhkhir yalhaqu bi al-asl' (a later description attaches to the original).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator (Ashraf al-Hidayah) declares Imam Muhammad's view wrong (ghalat). Therefore, the relied-upon position is that of the Shaykhayn: the kafalah bi al-mal is valid.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

کتاب الکفالہ

اشرف الہدایہ جلد نہم

کفیل نے بطور رشوت مکفول الہ کو ایک سودینار دیئے تاکہ مکفول الہ یعنی قرض خواہ مکفول بنفسہ یعنی قرضدار کو ایک مدت تک مہلت دے دے اور رشوت کے طور پر چونکہ اپنے اوپر مال لازم کرنا صحیح نہ ہوا تو یہ کفالہ بالمال بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا۔ اس کو یوں بھی کہا جا سکتا ہے کہ کفیل نے اپنے اوپر جو مال مطلق لازم کیا ہے وہ بطور رشوت بھی ہو سکتا ہے اور وہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے جو مال مکفول بنفسہ یعنی قرضدار پر واجب تھا۔ پہلی صورت یعنی رشوت کی صورت میں مال لازم نہیں ہوتا اور دوسری صورت یعنی ضامن ہونے کی صورت میں مال لازم ہو جاتا ہے۔ پس کفیل پر مال لازم ہونے میں شک ہو گیا۔ درآنحالیکہ کفیل پر پہلے سے مال لازم نہیں تھا۔ اور شک کی وجہ سے غیر لازم مال چونکہ لازم نہیں ہوتا، اس لیے یہاں بھی کفیل پر مال لازم نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب کفیل پر مال لازم نہیں ہوا تو کفالہ بالمال بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا۔

دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ مسئلہ مذکورہ میں کفالہ بالنفس ہی باطل ہے کیونکہ کفالہ بالنفس کا صحیح ہونا اس پر موقوف ہے کہ مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ کا دعویٰ قرض صحیح ہو اور قرض کا دعویٰ اس وقت صحیح ہوتا ہے جبکہ قرض کے مال کی مقدار بھی معلوم ہو اور اس کی صفت یعنی جید یا ردی ہونا بھی معلوم ہو۔ اور اس جگہ مسئلہ یہ فرض کیا گیا ہے کہ مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ نے مال قرض کا وصف جید یا ردی ہونا بیان نہیں کیا۔ اور جب وصف بیان نہیں کیا تو بغیر بیان وصف کے مدعی بہ مجہول ہو گیا اور مدعی بہ مجہول ہونے کی صورت میں دعویٰ صحیح نہیں ہوتا، لہٰذا قرض خواہ کا دعویٰ قرض صحیح نہ ہوا۔ اور جب قرض خواہ کا دعویٰ صحیح نہ ہوا تو کفیل پر قرضدار یعنی مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر کرنا بھی واجب نہ ہوا۔ اور جب کفیل پر قرضدار یعنی مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر کرنا واجب نہ ہوا تو کفالہ بالنفس بھی صحیح نہ ہوا۔ اور جب کفالہ بالنفس صحیح نہیں ہے تو کفالہ بالمال بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ کفالہ بالمال، اس مسئلہ میں کفالہ بالنفس پر مبنی ہے۔ پس جب بنی علیہ ہی صحیح نہیں ہے تو بنی کس طرح صحیح ہوگا۔

اس کے برخلاف اگر قرض خواہ نے قرض کا دعویٰ کرتے وقت مدعی بہ یعنی مال قرض کا وصف بھی بیان کر دیا ہوتو کفالہ بالنفس صحیح ہو جائے گا۔ اور جب کفالہ بالنفس صحیح ہے تو کفالہ بالمال جو اس پر مبنی ہے وہ بھی درست ہو جائے گا۔

شیخین کی دلیل جو امام محمد کی پہلی دلیل کا جواب بھی ہے یہ ہے کہ کفیل کے قول "على أنه إن لم یوف به غداً فعليه المائة" میں المائة یعنی ایک سودینار معرف باللام ہے اور لام بھی عہد خارجی کا ہے جس کا مدخول معین ہوتا ہے۔ پس اس المائة سے مراد وہ سودینار ہیں جو مکفول بنفسہ یعنی قرضدار پر واجب ہیں۔ اور جب المائة سے مراد وہی سودینار ہیں تو یہ سودینار رشوت ہونے سے خارج ہو گئے۔ اور جب رشوت کا احتمال نہ رہا تو یہ مال کفالہ کی وجہ سے کفیل پر لازم ہوگا۔ اور جب کفیل پر لازم ہو گیا تو یہ کفالہ بھی درست ہو جائے گا۔

شیخین کی دوسری دلیل جو امام محمد کی دوسری دلیل کا جواب بھی ہے یہ ہے کہ قرض خواہ نے جب قرضدار پر ایک سو دینار کا دعویٰ کیا اور اس کا وصف یعنی جید یا ردی ہونا بیان نہیں کیا یہاں تک کہ تیسرا آدمی قرضدار کی طرف سے کفیل بنفسہ ہو گیا، پھر مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ نے کفالہ کے بعد مال کا وصف بیان کیا تو اس میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے کیونکہ لوگوں کی عادت یہ ہے کہ وہ مجلس قاضی کے علاوہ میں مدعی علیہ کے حیلوں کو دور کرنے کے لیے دعاوی کو مجمل رکھتے ہیں اور مجلس قاضی میں جب ضرورت پڑتی ہے تو اوصاف وغیرہ بیان کر کے اس کے تمام گوشوں کو اجاگر کرتے ہیں۔ پس بیان اوصاف پر اعتماد کر کے مجمل دعویٰ صحیح ہو جاتا ہے۔ پھر جب مدعی یعنی قرض خواہ نے ایک سو دینار مال کا وصف بیان کر دیا تو یہ بیان وصف اصل دعویٰ کے ساتھ لاحق ہو جاتا ہے۔ یعنی ایسا ہو جاتا ہے گویا اس نے دعویٰ کرتے وقت ہی مال کا وصف بھی بیان کر دیا تھا۔ اور جب دعویٰ کرتے وقت مال کا وصف بھی بیان کر دیا گیا تو مال یعنی مدعی بہ مجہول نہ رہا اور جب مدعی بہ مجہول نہ رہا تو دعویٰ مال صحیح ہو گیا۔ اور جب مال کا دعویٰ صحیح ہو گیا تو کفیل پر مکفول بنفسہ یعنی قرضدار کو حاضر کرنا بھی واجب ہو گیا۔ اور جب کفیل پر مکفول بنفسہ کو حاضر کرنا واجب ہو گیا تو کفالہ بالنفس بھی صحیح ہو گیا۔ اور جب کفالہ بالنفس صحیح ہو گیا تو کفالہ بالمال جو اس پر مرتب ہے وہ بھی صحیح ہو جائے گا۔ پس امام محمد کا یہ فرمانا کہ اگر دعویٰ کرتے وقت قرض خواہ نے مال کا وصف بیان نہ کیا تو کفالہ بالمال اور کفالہ بالنفس صحیح نہ ہوگا، غلط ہے۔
Book of Suretyship. Ashraf al-Hidayah Volume Nine: The surety gave a hundred dinars as a bribe to the creditor so that the creditor would give the debtor a respite for a period. Since making wealth obligatory on oneself as a bribe is not valid, this suretyship for wealth is also not valid. It can also be said that the wealth that the surety made absolutely obligatory on himself could be a bribe or it could be the wealth that was due from the debtor. In the first case (bribe) the wealth is not obligatory, and in the second case (suretyship) the wealth becomes obligatory. Thus there is doubt about the wealth being obligatory on the surety, whereas previously the wealth was not obligatory on the surety. And due to doubt, non-obligatory wealth does not become obligatory. Therefore, the wealth will not be obligatory on the surety here. And when the wealth is not obligatory on the surety, the suretyship for wealth will not be valid. The second proof is that in the mentioned issue, the suretyship for the person is itself invalid because the validity of suretyship for the person depends on the validity of the creditor's claim of debt, and the claim of debt is valid only when the amount of the debt is known and its quality (good or bad) is known. In this case, it is assumed that the creditor did not describe the quality of the debt. When the quality is not described, the claimed object becomes unknown, and with the claimed object unknown, the claim is not valid. Thus the creditor's claim of debt is not valid. When the creditor's claim is not valid, it is not obligatory on the surety to produce the debtor. And when it is not obligatory to produce the debtor, the suretyship for the person is not valid. And when the suretyship for the person is not valid, the suretyship for wealth will also not be valid, because in this issue the suretyship for wealth is based on the suretyship for the person. If the foundation is not valid, how can the structure be valid? Conversely, if the creditor had described the quality of the claimed object (the debt) at the time of claiming, then the suretyship for the person would be valid, and when the suretyship for the person is valid, the suretyship for wealth based on it would also be correct. The proof of the Shaykhayn, which is also the answer to Imam Muhammad's first proof, is that in the surety's statement 'on condition that if he does not bring him tomorrow, then upon him is the hundred', the word 'al-mi'ah' (the hundred) is defined with the definite article, and the lam is for external specification, its referent being determinate. So by 'al-mi'ah' is meant the hundred dinars that were due from the debtor. And when 'al-mi'ah' refers to those same dinars, these dinars are excluded from being a bribe. When the possibility of bribery is removed, this wealth becomes obligatory on the surety due to the suretyship, and when it is obligatory on the surety, this suretyship is valid. The second proof of the Shaykhayn, which is also the answer to Imam Muhammad's second proof, is that when the creditor claimed one hundred dinars from the debtor and did not describe its quality (good or bad), to the extent that a third person became a surety for the person on behalf of the debtor, then after the suretyship the creditor described the quality of the wealth, there is no harm in this because the habit of people is that outside the judge's court they keep claims summary to avoid the defendant's tricks, and in the judge's court when needed they describe the attributes and reveal all aspects. So by relying on the description of attributes, the summary claim becomes valid. Then when the creditor described the quality of the wealth, this description becomes attached to the original claim, as if he had described the quality of the wealth at the time of claiming. And when the quality was described at the time of claiming, the claimed object was not unknown, and when it was not unknown, the claim for wealth became valid. When the claim for wealth became valid, it became obligatory on the surety to produce the debtor. When it became obligatory, the suretyship for the person became valid, and when the suretyship for the person became valid, the suretyship for wealth based on it also becomes valid. So Imam Muhammad's statement that if the creditor did not describe the quality at the time of claiming, the suretyship for wealth and the suretyship for person are not valid, is incorrect.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a suretyship for debt (kafalah bi al-mal) when the surety (kafil) gives money to the creditor as a bribe to induce him to grant a delay to the debtor. The commentator explains two arguments raised by Imam Muhammad against the validity of such a kafalah, followed by the responses of the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) who uphold its validity. Imam Muhammad's first argument is that the money could be a bribe (no obligation) or the debt (obligation), creating doubt, so no obligation arises. The Shaykhayn respond that the definite article in the surety's statement identifies the money as the specific debt, removing doubt. Second, Imam Muhammad argues that the creditor's initial claim lacked quality description, making it invalid, and thus the kafalah bi al-nafs (and consequently kafalah bi al-mal) fails. The Shaykhayn reply that it is customary to give a summary claim outside court and detail it in court; the later description retroactively perfects the claim. The commentator declares Imam Muhammad's view wrong and the Shaykhayn's view correct.

Key Terms
کفالہ بالمال (kafalah bi al-mal): Suretyship for wealth/debt: a contract where a person (kafil) guarantees payment of a debt if the debtor fails to pay.
کفالہ بالنفس (kafalah bi al-nafs): Suretyship for the person: a guarantee to produce the debtor (or accused) before the court on a specified date.
مکفول بہ (makful bihi): The object of the suretyship, i.e., the debt (in kafalah bi al-mal) or the person to be produced (in kafalah bi al-nafs).
مدعی بہ (mudda'a bihi): The claimed object or right; in this context, the debt amount and its quality.
وصف (wasf): Description or attribute of the debt, such as being of good or bad quality (jīd or radī).
رشوت (rishwah): Bribe; a payment made to influence the creditor to grant a delay.
معرف باللام (mu'arraf bi al-lam): Defined by the definite article 'al-', indicating a specific known referent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what is the effect of the creditor describing the quality of the debt after the kafalah is concluded?
A) The description is irrelevant and the kafalah remains invalid.
B) The description attaches retroactively to the original claim, making it valid.
C) The kafalah bi al-mal becomes valid only if the description is made before the kafalah.
D) The kafalah becomes valid only if the debtor consents.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a suretyship is valid even if the money was given as a bribe, as long as the wording identifies it as the debt, and a later description of debt quality cures an initially vague claim.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate

Timing of Property Description in Kafalah: Refuting Imam Muhammad
Refutation of Imam Muhammad's view on property description in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects Imam Muhammad's condition that the property must be described at the time of claim for the validity of kafalah bi al-mal and kafalah bi al-nafs.

Core Idea
Describing property at the time of claim suffices; earlier description not required.

This passage addresses an internal Hanafi disagreement concerning the conditions for a valid kafalah (suretyship) contract. Imam Muhammad held that if the creditor does not describe the property when filing the claim, both kafalah bi al-mal and kafalah bi al-nafs are invalid. The author presents a logical chain refuting this, showing that once the property is described at the time of claim, the claim becomes known, which in turn validates both types of kafalah. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that description at the time of claim is sufficient, and Imam Muhammad's contrary view is rejected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi majority position
First, note that the majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that describing the property at the time of claim is sufficient for the validity of kafalah. Imam Muhammad's view is a minority position within the school.
2
State Imam Muhammad's contrary view
Imam Muhammad argues that if the creditor does not describe the property at the time of filing the claim, the kafalah bi al-mal and kafalah bi al-nafs are not valid. He treats description at the time of claim as a condition for validity.
3
Follow the author's logical chain
The author presents a sequential reasoning: when the property is described at the time of claim, it becomes known (ma'lum); a known claim is valid; a valid property claim obligates the guarantor to produce the debtor; this obligation makes kafalah bi al-nafs valid; and since kafalah bi al-mal is attached, it also becomes valid.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) based on the interdependence of the two types of kafalah.
4
Identify the conclusion and mufta bihi
The author concludes that Imam Muhammad's position is incorrect. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that kafalah is valid even if the property description is given at the time of claim, not necessarily before.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Imam Muhammad's view, a kafalah contract could be invalidated if the property is not described in the initial claim. Under the majority view, the contract remains valid as long as the property is described at any point during the lawsuit.
This protects the rights of creditors and avoids unnecessary invalidation of guarantees.
